 \xe2\x80\x9cLet us now praise famous men\xe2\x80\x9d--\r\n      Men of little showing--\r\n     For their work continueth,\r\n     And their work continueth,\r\n      Greater than their knowing.\r\n\r\n     Western wind and open surge\r\n      Tore us from our mothers;\r\n     Flung us on a naked shore\r\n     (Twelve bleak houses by the shore!\r\n     Seven summers by the shore!)\r\n      \xe2\x80\x99Mid two hundred brothers.\r\n\r\n     There we met with famous men\r\n      Set in office o\xe2\x80\x99er us.\r\n     And they beat on us with rods--\r\n     Faithfully with many rods--\r\n     Daily beat us on with rods--\r\n      For the love they bore us!\r\n\r\n     Out of Egypt unto Troy--\r\n      Over Himalaya--\r\n     Far and sure our bands have gone--\r\n     Hy--Brasil or Babylon,\r\n     Islands of the Southern Run,\r\n      And cities of Cathaia!\r\n\r\n     And we all praise famous men--\r\n      Ancients of the College;\r\n     For they taught us common sense---\r\n     Tried to teach us common sense--\r\n     Truth and God\xe2\x80\x99s Own Common Sense\r\n      Which is more than knowledge!\r\n\r\n     Each degree of Latitude\r\n      Strung about Creation\r\n     Seeth one (or more) of us,\r\n     (Of one muster all of us--\r\n     Of one master all of us--)\r\n      Keen in his vocation.\r\n\r\n     This we learned from famous men\r\n      Knowing not its uses\r\n     When they showed in daily work\r\n     Man must finish off his work--\r\n     Right or wrong, his daily work--\r\n      And without excuses.\r\n\r\n     Servants of the staff and chain,\r\n      Mine and fuse and grapnel--\r\n     Some before the face of Kings,\r\n     Stand before the face of Kings;\r\n     Bearing gifts to divers Kings--\r\n      Gifts of Case and Shrapnel.\r\n\r\n     This we learned from famous men\r\n      Teaching in our borders.\r\n     Who declare\xe2\x80\x99d it was best,\r\n     Safest, easiest and best--\r\n     Expeditious, wise and best--\r\n      To obey your orders.\r\n\r\n     Some beneath the further stars\r\n      Bear the greater burden.\r\n     Set to serve the lands they rule,\r\n     (Save he serve no man may rule)\r\n     Serve and love the lands they rule;\r\n      Seeking praise nor guerdon.\r\n\r\n     This we learned from famous men\r\n      Knowing not we learned it.\r\n     Only, as the years went by--\r\n     Lonely, as the years went by--\r\n     Far from help as years went by\r\n      Plainer we discerned it.\r\n\r\n     Wherefore praise we famous men\r\n      From whose bays we borrow--\r\n     They that put aside Today--\r\n     All the joys of their Today--\r\n     And with toil of their Today\r\n      Bought for us Tomorrow!\r\n\r\n     _Bless and praise we famous men\r\n      Men of little showing!\r\n     For their work continueth\r\n     And their work continueth\r\n     Broad and deep continueth\r\n      Great beyond their knowing!_\r\n\r\n     Copyright, 1899. by Rudyard Kipling\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCONTENTS\r\n\r\n\r\n     I.    \xe2\x80\x9cIN AMBUSH.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n     II.   SLAVES OF THE LAMP--PART I.\r\n     III.  AN UNSAVORY INTERLUDE\r\n     IV.   THE IMPRESSIONISTS\r\n     V.    THE MORAL REFORMERS\r\n     VI.   A LITTLE PREP.\r\n     VII.  THE FLAG OF THEIR COUNTRY\r\n     VIII. THE LAST TERM\r\n     IX.   SLAVES OF THE LAMP--PART II.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIN AMBUSH.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nIn summer all right-minded boys built huts in the furze-hill behind the\r\nCollege--little lairs whittled out of the heart of the prickly bushes,\r\nfull of stumps, odd root-ends, and spikes, but, since they were strictly\r\nforbidden, palaces of delight. And for the fifth summer in succession,\r\nStalky, McTurk, and Beetle (this was before they reached the dignity of\r\na study) had built like beavers a place of retreat and meditation, where\r\nthey smoked.\r\n\r\nNow, there was nothing in their characters as known to Mr. Prout,\r\ntheir house-master, at all commanding respect; nor did Foxy, the\r\nsubtle red-haired school Sergeant, trust them. His business was to wear\r\ntennis-shoes, carry binoculars, and swoop hawklike upon evil boys. Had\r\nhe taken the field alone, that hut would have been raided, for Foxy\r\nknew the manners of his quarry; but Providence moved Mr. Prout,\r\nwhose school-name, derived from the size of his feet, was Hoofer, to\r\ninvestigate on his own account; and it was the cautious Stalky who\r\nfound the track of his pugs on the very floor of their lair one peaceful\r\nafternoon when Stalky would fain have forgotten Prout and his works in\r\na volume of Surtees and a new briar-wood pipe. Crusoe, at sight of the\r\nfootprint, did not act more swiftly than Stalky. He removed the pipes,\r\nswept up all loose match-ends, and departed to warn Beetle and McTurk.\r\n\r\nBut it was characteristic of the boy that he did not approach his allies\r\ntill he had met and conferred with little Hartopp, President of the\r\nNatural History Society, an institution which Stalky held in contempt.\r\nHartopp was more than surprised when the boy meekly, as he knew how,\r\nbegged to propose himself, Beetle, and McTurk as candidates; confessed\r\nto a long-smothered interest in first-flowerings, early butterflies, and\r\nnew arrivals, and volunteered, if Mr. Hartopp saw fit, to enter on the\r\nnew life at once. Being a master, Hartopp was suspicious; but he was\r\nalso an enthusiast, and his gentle little soul had been galled by\r\nchance-heard remarks from the three, and specially Beetle. So he was\r\ngracious to that repentant sinner, and entered the three names in his\r\nbook.\r\n\r\nThen, and not till then, did Stalky seek Beetle and McTurk in their\r\nhouse form-room. They were stowing away books for a quiet afternoon in\r\nthe furze, which they called the \xe2\x80\x9cwuzzy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll up,\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky, serenely. \xe2\x80\x9cI spotted Heffy\xe2\x80\x99s fairy feet round our\r\nhut after dinner. \xe2\x80\x99Blessing they\xe2\x80\x99re so big.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCon-found! Did you hide our pipes?\xe2\x80\x9d said Beetle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, no. Left \xe2\x80\x99em in the middle of the hut, of course. What a blind ass\r\nyou are, Beetle! D\xe2\x80\x99you think nobody thinks but yourself? Well, we can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nuse the hut any more. Hoofer will be watchin\xe2\x80\x99 it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Bother! Likewise blow!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d said McTurk thoughtfully, unpacking the\r\nvolumes with which his chest was cased. The boys carried their libraries\r\nbetween their belt and their collar. \xe2\x80\x9cNice job! This means we\xe2\x80\x99re under\r\nsuspicion for the rest of the term.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy? All that Heffy has found is a hut. He and Foxy will watch it. It\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nnothing to do with us; only we mustn\xe2\x80\x99t be seen that way for a bit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, and where else are we to go?\xe2\x80\x9d said Beetle. \xe2\x80\x9cYou chose that place,\r\ntoo--an\xe2\x80\x99--an\xe2\x80\x99 I wanted to read this afternoon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nStalky sat on a desk drumming his heels on the form.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re a despondin\xe2\x80\x99 brute, Beetle. Sometimes I think I shall have to\r\ndrop you altogether. Did you ever know your Uncle Stalky forget you yet?\r\n_His rebus infectis_--after I\xe2\x80\x99d seen Heffy\xe2\x80\x99s man-tracks marchin\xe2\x80\x99\r\nround our hut, I found little Hartopp--_destricto ense_--wavin\xe2\x80\x99 a\r\nbutterfly-net. I conciliated Hartopp. \xe2\x80\x99Told him that you\xe2\x80\x99d read papers\r\nto the Bug-hunters if he\xe2\x80\x99d let you join, Beetle. \xe2\x80\x99Told him you liked\r\nbutterflies, Turkey. Anyhow, I soothed the Hartoffles, and we\xe2\x80\x99re\r\nBug-hunters now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the good of that?\xe2\x80\x9d said Beetle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Turkey, kick him!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn the interests of science bounds were largely relaxed for the members\r\nof the Natural History Society. They could wander, if they kept clear\r\nof all houses, practically where they chose; Mr. Hartopp holding himself\r\nresponsible for their good conduct.\r\n\r\nBeetle began to see this as McTurk began the kicking.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m an ass, Stalky!\xe2\x80\x9d he said, guarding the afflicted part. \xe2\x80\x9c_Pax_,\r\nTurkey. I\xe2\x80\x99m an ass.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t stop, Turkey. Isn\xe2\x80\x99t your Uncle Stalky a great man?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGreat man,\xe2\x80\x9d said Beetle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll the same bug-huntin\xe2\x80\x99s a filthy business,\xe2\x80\x9d said McTurk. \xe2\x80\x9cHow the\r\ndeuce does one begin?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis way,\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky, turning to some fags\xe2\x80\x99 lockers behind him. \xe2\x80\x9cFags\r\nare dabs at Natural History. Here\xe2\x80\x99s young Braybrooke\xe2\x80\x99s botany-case.\xe2\x80\x9d He\r\nflung out a tangle of decayed roots and adjusted the slide. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Gives one\r\nno end of a professional air, I think. Here\xe2\x80\x99s Clay Minor\xe2\x80\x99s geological\r\nhammer. Beetle can carry that. Turkey, you\xe2\x80\x99d better covet a\r\nbutterfly-net from somewhere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m blowed if I do,\xe2\x80\x9d said McTurk, simply, with immense feeling.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBeetle, give me the hammer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right. _I_\xe2\x80\x99m not proud. Chuck us down that net on top of the\r\nlockers, Stalky.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s all right. It\xe2\x80\x99s a collapsible jamboree, too. Beastly luxurious\r\ndogs these fags are. Built like a fishin\xe2\x80\x99-rod. \xe2\x80\x99Pon my sainted Sam,\r\nbut we look the complete Bug-hunters! Now, listen to your Uncle Stalky!\r\nWe\xe2\x80\x99re goin\xe2\x80\x99 along the cliffs after butterflies. Very few chaps come\r\nthere. We\xe2\x80\x99re goin\xe2\x80\x99 to leg it, too. You\xe2\x80\x99d better leave your book behind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot much!\xe2\x80\x9d said Beetle, firmly. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not goin\xe2\x80\x99 to be done out of my fun\r\nfor a lot of filthy butterflies.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you\xe2\x80\x99ll sweat horrid. You\xe2\x80\x99d better carry my Jorrocks. \xe2\x80\x99Twon\xe2\x80\x99t make\r\nyou any hotter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey all sweated; for Stalky led them at a smart trot west away along\r\nthe cliffs under the furze-hills, crossing combe after gorzy combe. They\r\ntook no heed to flying rabbits or fluttering fritillaries, and all that\r\nTurkey said of geology was utterly unquotable.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre we going to Clovelly?\xe2\x80\x9d he puffed at last, and they flung themselves\r\ndown on the short, springy turf between the drone of the sea below and\r\nthe light summer wind among the inland trees. They were looking into a\r\ncombe half full of old, high furze in gay bloom that ran up to a fringe\r\nof brambles and a dense wood of mixed timber and hollies. It was as\r\nthough one-half the combe were filled with golden fire to the cliff\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nedge. The side nearest to them was open grass, and fairly bristled with\r\nnotice-boards.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFee-rocious old cove, this,\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky, reading the nearest.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98_Prosecuted with the utmost rigour of the law_. _G. M. Dabney, Col.,\r\nJ.P._,\xe2\x80\x99 an\xe2\x80\x99 all the rest of it. \xe2\x80\x99Don\xe2\x80\x99t seem to me that any chap in his\r\nsenses would trespass here, does it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve got to prove damage \xe2\x80\x99fore you can prosecute for anything! \xe2\x80\x99Can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nprosecute for trespass,\xe2\x80\x9d said McTurk, whose father held many acres in\r\nIreland. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s all rot!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGlad of that, \xe2\x80\x99cause this looks like what we wanted. Not straight\r\nacross, Beetle, you blind lunatic! Anyone could spot us half a mile off.\r\nThis way; and furl up your beastly butterfly-net.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBeetle disconnected the ring, thrust the net into a pocket, shut up\r\nthe handle to a two-foot stave, and slid the cane-ring round his waist.\r\nStalky led inland to the wood, which was, perhaps, a quarter of a mile\r\nfrom the sea, and reached the fringe of the brambles.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Now_ we can get straight down through the furze, and never show up\r\nat all,\xe2\x80\x9d said the tactician. \xe2\x80\x9cBeetle, go ahead and explore. Snf! Snf!\r\nBeastly stink of fox somewhere!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOn all fours, save when he clung to his spectacles, Beetle wormed into\r\nthe gorse, and presently announced between grunts of pain that he had\r\nfound a very fair fox-track. This was well for Beetle, since Stalky\r\npinched him _a tergo_. Down that tunnel they crawled. It was evidently\r\na highway for the inhabitants of the combe; and, to their inexpressible\r\njoy, ended, at the very edge of the cliff, in a few square feet of dry\r\nturf walled and roofed with impenetrable gorse.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy gum! There isn\xe2\x80\x99t a single thing to do except lie down,\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky,\r\nreturning a knife to his pocket. \xe2\x80\x9cLook here!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe parted the tough stems before him, and it was as a window opened on\r\na far view of Lundy, and the deep sea sluggishly nosing the pebbles a\r\ncouple of hundred feet below. They could hear young jackdaws squawking\r\non the ledges, the hiss and jabber of a nest of hawks somewhere out of\r\nsight; and, with great deliberation, Stalky spat on to the back of a\r\nyoung rabbit sunning himself far down where only a cliff-rabbit could\r\nhave found foot-hold. Great gray and black gulls screamed against the\r\njackdaws; the heavy-scented acres of bloom round them were alive with\r\nlow-nesting birds, singing or silent as the shadow of the wheeling hawks\r\npassed and returned; and on the naked turf across the combe rabbits\r\nthumped and frolicked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhew! What a place! Talk of natural history; this is it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky,\r\nfilling himself a pipe. \xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t it scrumptious? Good old sea!\xe2\x80\x9d He spat\r\nagain approvingly, and was silent.\r\n\r\nMcTurk and Beetle had taken out their books and were lying on their\r\nstomachs, chin in hand. The sea snored and gurgled; the birds, scattered\r\nfor the moment by these new animals, returned to their businesses, and\r\nthe boys read on in the rich, warm, sleepy silence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHullo, here\xe2\x80\x99s a keeper,\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky, shutting \xe2\x80\x9cHandley Cross\xe2\x80\x9d\r\ncautiously, and peering through the jungle. A man with a gun appeared\r\non the sky-line to the east. \xe2\x80\x9cConfound him, he\xe2\x80\x99s going to sit down.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99d swear we were poachin\xe2\x80\x99, too,\xe2\x80\x9d said Beetle. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the good of\r\npheasants\xe2\x80\x99 eggs? They\xe2\x80\x99re always addled, too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMight as well get up to the wood, _I_ think,\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky. \xe2\x80\x9cWe don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nwant G. M. Dabney, Col., J.P., to be bothered about us so soon. Up the\r\nwuzzy and keep quiet! He may have followed us, you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBeetle was already far up the tunnel. They heard him gasp indescribably:\r\nthere was the crash of a heavy body leaping through the furze.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAie! yeou little red rascal. I see yeou!\xe2\x80\x9d The keeper threw the gun to\r\nhis shoulder, and fired both barrels in their direction. The pellets\r\ndusted the dry stems round them as a big fox plunged between Stalky\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nlegs, and ran over the cliff-edge.\r\n\r\nThey said nothing till they reached the wood, torn, disheveled, hot, but\r\nunseen.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNarrow squeak,\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll swear some of the pellets went\r\nthrough my hair.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you see him?\xe2\x80\x9d said Beetle. \xe2\x80\x9cI almost put my hand on him. Wasn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nhe a wopper! Didn\xe2\x80\x99t he stink! Hullo, Turkey, what\xe2\x80\x99s the matter? Are you\r\nhit?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMcTurk\xe2\x80\x99s lean face had turned pearly white; his mouth, generally half\r\nopen, was tight shut, and his eyes blazed. They had never seen him like\r\nthis save once in a sad time of civil war.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you know that that was just as bad as murder?\xe2\x80\x9d he said, in a grating\r\nvoice, as he brushed prickles from his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, he didn\xe2\x80\x99t hit us,\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky. \xe2\x80\x9cI think it was rather a lark.\r\nHere, where are you going?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m going up to the house, if there is one,\xe2\x80\x9d said McTurk, pushing\r\nthrough the hollies. \xe2\x80\x9cI am going to tell this Colonel Dabney.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you crazy? He\xe2\x80\x99ll swear it served us jolly well right. He\xe2\x80\x99ll report\r\nus. It\xe2\x80\x99ll be a public lickin\xe2\x80\x99. Oh, Turkey, don\xe2\x80\x99t be an ass! Think of\r\nus!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou fool!\xe2\x80\x9d said McTurk, turning savagely. \xe2\x80\x9cD\xe2\x80\x99you suppose I\xe2\x80\x99m thinkin\xe2\x80\x99\r\nof _us_? It\xe2\x80\x99s the keeper.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s cracked,\xe2\x80\x9d said Beetle, miserably, as they followed. Indeed, this\r\nwas a new Turkey--a haughty, angular, nose-lifted Turkey--whom they\r\naccompanied through a shrubbery on to a lawn, where a white-whiskered\r\nold gentleman with a cleek was alternately putting and blaspheming\r\nvigorously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you Colonel Dabney?\xe2\x80\x9d McTurk began in this new creaking voice of\r\nhis.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI--I am, and--\xe2\x80\x9d his eyes traveled up and down the boy--\xe2\x80\x9cwho--what the\r\ndevil d\xe2\x80\x99you want? Ye\xe2\x80\x99ve been disturbing my pheasants. Don\xe2\x80\x99t attempt to\r\ndeny it. Ye needn\xe2\x80\x99t laugh at it.\xe2\x80\x9d (McTurk\xe2\x80\x99s not too lovely features had\r\ntwisted themselves into a horrible sneer at the word pheasant.) \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nbeen birds\xe2\x80\x99-nesting. You needn\xe2\x80\x99t hide your hat. I can see that you\r\nbelong to the College. Don\xe2\x80\x99t attempt to deny it. Ye do! Your name\r\nand number at once, sir. Ye want to speak to me--Eh? You saw my\r\nnotice-boards? Must have. Don\xe2\x80\x99t attempt to deny it. Ye did! Damnable, oh\r\ndamnable!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe choked with emotion. McTurk\xe2\x80\x99s heel tapped the lawn and he stuttered a\r\nlittle--two sure signs that he was losing his temper. But why should he,\r\nthe offender, be angry?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLo-look here, sir. Do--do you shoot foxes? Because, if you don\xe2\x80\x99t, your\r\nkeeper does. We\xe2\x80\x99ve seen him! I do-don\xe2\x80\x99t care what you call us--but it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nan awful thing. It\xe2\x80\x99s the ruin of good feelin\xe2\x80\x99 among neighbors. A ma-man\r\nought to say once and for all how he stands about preservin\xe2\x80\x99. It\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nworse than murder, because there\xe2\x80\x99s no legal remedy.\xe2\x80\x9d McTurk was quoting\r\nconfusedly from his father, while the old gentleman made noises in his\r\nthroat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you know who I am?\xe2\x80\x9d he gurgled at last; Stalky and Beetle quaking.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sorr, nor do I care if ye belonged to the Castle itself. Answer me\r\nnow, as one gentleman to another. Do ye shoot foxes or do ye not?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd four years before Stalky and Beetle had carefully kicked McTurk out\r\nof his Irish dialect! Assuredly he had gone mad or taken a sunstroke,\r\nand as assuredly he would be slain--once by the old gentleman and once\r\nby the Head. A public licking for the three was the least they could\r\nexpect. Yet--if their eyes and ears were to be trusted--the old\r\ngentleman had collapsed. It might be a lull before the storm, but--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not.\xe2\x80\x9d He was still gurgling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you must sack your keeper. He\xe2\x80\x99s not fit to live in the same county\r\nwith a God-fearin\xe2\x80\x99 fox. An\xe2\x80\x99 a vixen, too--at this time o\xe2\x80\x99 year!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid ye come up on purpose to tell me this?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course I did, ye silly man,\xe2\x80\x9d with a stamp of the foot. \xe2\x80\x9cWould you\r\nnot have done as much for me if you\xe2\x80\x99d seen that thing happen on my land,\r\nnow?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nForgotten--forgotten was the College and the decency due to elders!\r\nMcTurk was treading again the barren purple mountains of the rainy\r\nWest coast, where in his holidays he was viceroy of four thousand naked\r\nacres, only son of a three-hundred-year-old house, lord of a crazy\r\nfishing-boat, and the idol of his father\xe2\x80\x99s shiftless tenantry. It was\r\nthe landed man speaking to his equal--deep calling to deep--and the old\r\ngentleman acknowledged the cry.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI apologize,\xe2\x80\x9d said he. \xe2\x80\x9cI apologize unreservedly--to you, and to the\r\nOld Country. Now, will you be good enough to tell me your story?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe were in your combe,\xe2\x80\x9d McTurk began, and he told his tale alternately\r\nas a schoolboy and, when the iniquity of the thing overcame him, as an\r\nindignant squire; concluding: \xe2\x80\x9cSo you see he must be in the habit of\r\nit. I--we---one never wants to accuse a neighbor\xe2\x80\x99s man; but I took the\r\nliberty in this case--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see. Quite so. For a reason ye had. Infamous---oh, infamous!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe two had fallen into step beside each other on the lawn, and Colonel\r\nDabney was talking as one man to another. \xe2\x80\x9cThis comes of promoting a\r\nfisherman--a fisherman--from his lobster-pots. It\xe2\x80\x99s enough to ruin the\r\nreputation of an archangel. Don\xe2\x80\x99t attempt to deny it. It is! Your father\r\nhas brought you up well. He has. I\xe2\x80\x99d much like the pleasure of his\r\nacquaintance. Very much, indeed. And these young gentlemen? English\r\nthey are. Don\xe2\x80\x99t attempt to deny it. They came up with you, too?\r\nExtraordinary! Extraordinary, now! In the present state of education I\r\nshouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have thought any three boys would be well enough grounded.\r\nBut out of the mouths of--No--no! Not that by any odds. Don\xe2\x80\x99t attempt to\r\ndeny it. Ye\xe2\x80\x99re not! Sherry always catches me under the liver, but--beer,\r\nnow? Eh? What d\xe2\x80\x99you say to beer, and something to eat? It\xe2\x80\x99s long since\r\nI was a boy--abominable nuisances; but exceptions prove the rule. And a\r\nvixen, too!\xe2\x80\x9d They were fed on the terrace by a gray-haired housekeeper.\r\nStalky and Beetle merely ate, but McTurk with bright eyes continued a\r\nfree and lofty discourse; and ever the old gentleman treated him as a\r\nbrother.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear man, of _course_ ye can come again. Did I not say exceptions\r\nprove the rule? The lower combe? Man, dear, anywhere ye please, so long\r\nas you do not disturb my pheasants. The two are not incompatible. Don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nattempt to deny it. They\xe2\x80\x99re not! I\xe2\x80\x99ll never allow another gun, though.\r\nCome and go as ye please. I\xe2\x80\x99ll not see you, and ye needn\xe2\x80\x99t see me. Ye\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nbeen well brought up. Another glass of beer, now? I tell you a fisherman\r\nhe was and a fisherman he shall be to-night again. He shall! Wish\r\nI could drown him. I\xe2\x80\x99ll convoy you to the Lodge. My people are not\r\nprecisely--ah--broke to boy, but they\xe2\x80\x99ll know you again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe dismissed them with many compliments by the high Lodge-gate in the\r\nsplit-oak park palings and they stood still; even Stalky, who had played\r\nsecond, not to say a dumb, fiddle, regarding McTurk as one from another\r\nworld. The two glasses of strong home-brewed had brought a melancholy\r\nupon the boy, for, slowly strolling with his hands in his pockets, he\r\ncrooned:--\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Paddy dear, and did ye hear the news that\xe2\x80\x99s goin\xe2\x80\x99 round?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nUnder other circumstances Stalky and Beetle would have fallen upon him,\r\nfor that song was barred utterly--anathema--the sin of witchcraft. But\r\nseeing what he had wrought, they danced round him in silence, waiting\r\ntill it pleased him to touch earth.\r\n\r\nThe tea-bell rang when they were still half a mile from College. McTurk\r\nshivered and came out of dreams. The glory of his holiday estate had\r\nleft him. He was a Colleger of the College, speaking English once more.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTurkey, it was immense!\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky, generously. \xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t know you\r\nhad it in you. You\xe2\x80\x99ve got us a hut for the rest of the term, where\r\nwe simply _can\xe2\x80\x99t_ be collared. Fids! Fids! Oh, Fids! I gloat! Hear me\r\ngloat!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey spun wildly on their heels, jodeling after the accepted manner of\r\na \xe2\x80\x9cgloat,\xe2\x80\x9d which is not unremotely allied to the primitive man\xe2\x80\x99s song of\r\ntriumph, and dropped down the hill by the path from the gasometer just\r\nin time to meet their house-master, who had spent the afternoon watching\r\ntheir abandoned hut in the \xe2\x80\x9cwuzzy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nUnluckily, all Mr. Prout\xe2\x80\x99s imagination leaned to the darker side of\r\nlife, and he looked on those young-eyed cherubims most sourly. Boys that\r\nhe understood attended house-matches and could be accounted for at\r\nany moment. But he had heard McTurk openly deride cricket--even\r\nhouse-matches; Beetle\xe2\x80\x99s views on the honor of the house he knew were\r\nincendiary; and he could never tell when the soft and smiling Stalky was\r\nlaughing at him. Consequently--since human nature is what it is--those\r\nboys had been doing wrong somewhere. He hoped it was nothing very\r\nserious, but...\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Ti-ra-ra-la-i-tu_! I gloat! Hear me!\xe2\x80\x9d Stalky, still on his heels,\r\nwhirled like a dancing dervish to the dining-hall.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Ti-ra-la-la-i-tu_! I gloat! Hear me!\xe2\x80\x9d Beetle spun behind him with\r\noutstretched arms.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Ti-ra-la-la-i-tu_! I gloat! Hear me!\xe2\x80\x9d McTurk\xe2\x80\x99s voice cracked.\r\n\r\nNow was there or was there not a distinct flavor of beer as they shot\r\npast Mr. Prout?\r\n\r\nHe was unlucky in that his conscience as a house-master impelled him to\r\nconsult his associates. Had he taken his pipe and his troubles to\r\nlittle Hartopp\xe2\x80\x99s rooms he would, perhaps, have been saved confusion, for\r\nHartopp believed in boys, and knew something about them. His fate led\r\nhim to King, a fellow house-master, no friend of his, but a zealous\r\nhater of Stalky & Co.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh-haa!\xe2\x80\x9d said King, rubbing his hands when the tale was told. \xe2\x80\x9cCurious!\r\nNow _my_ house never dream of doing these things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you see I\xe2\x80\x99ve no proof, exactly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cProof? With the egregious Beetle! As if one wanted it! I suppose it\r\nis not impossible for the Sergeant to supply it? Foxy is considered\r\nat least a match for any evasive boy in my house. Of course they were\r\nsmoking and drinking somewhere. That type of boy always does. They think\r\nit manly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut they\xe2\x80\x99ve no following in the school, and they are\r\ndistinctly--er--brutal to their juniors,\xe2\x80\x9d said Prout, who had from a\r\ndistance seen Beetle return, with interest, his butterfly-net to a\r\ntearful fag.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! They consider themselves superior to ordinary delights.\r\nSelf-sufficient little animals! There\xe2\x80\x99s something in McTurk\xe2\x80\x99s Hibernian\r\nsneer that would make _me_ a little annoyed. And they are so careful\r\nto avoid all overt acts, too. It\xe2\x80\x99s sheer calculated insolence. I am\r\nstrongly opposed, as you know, to interfering with another man\xe2\x80\x99s house;\r\nbut they need a lesson, Prout. They need a sharp lesson, if only to\r\nbring down their over-weening self-conceit. Were I you, I should devote\r\nmyself for a week to their little performances. Boys of that order--and\r\nI may flatter myself, but I think I know boys--don\xe2\x80\x99t join the\r\nBug-hunters for love. Tell the Sergeant to keep his eye open; and, of\r\ncourse, in my peregrinations I may casually keep mine open, too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Ti-ra-la-la-i-tu_! I gloat! Hear me!\xe2\x80\x9d far down the corridor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDisgusting!\xe2\x80\x9d said King. \xe2\x80\x9cWhere do they pick up these obscene noises?\r\nOne sharp lesson is what they want.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe boys did not concern themselves with lessons for the next few days.\r\nThey had all Colonel Dabney\xe2\x80\x99s estate to play with, and they explored it\r\nwith the stealth of Red Indians and the accuracy of burglars. They could\r\nenter either by the Lodge-gates on the upper road--they were careful to\r\ningratiate themselves with the Lodge-keeper and his wife--drop down into\r\nthe combe, and return along the cliffs; or they could begin at the combe\r\nand climb up into the road.\r\n\r\nThey were careful not to cross the Colonel\xe2\x80\x99s path--he had served his\r\nturn, and they would not out-wear their welcome--nor did they show up on\r\nthe sky-line when they could move in cover. The shelter of the gorze\r\nby the cliff-edge was their chosen retreat. Beetle christened it the\r\nPleasant Isle of Aves, for the peace and the shelter of it; and here,\r\nthe pipes and tobacco once cach\xc3\xa9d in a convenient ledge an arm\xe2\x80\x99s length\r\ndown the cliff, their position was legally unassailable.\r\n\r\nFor, observe, Colonel Dabney had not invited them to enter his house.\r\nTherefore, they did not need to ask specific leave to go visiting; and\r\nschool rules were strict on that point. He had merely thrown open his\r\ngrounds to them; and, since they were lawful Bug-hunters, their extended\r\nbounds ran up to his notice-boards in the combe and his Lodge-gates on\r\nthe hill.\r\n\r\nThey were amazed at their own virtue.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd even if it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky, flat on his back, staring into the\r\nblue. \xe2\x80\x9cEven suppose we were miles out of bounds, no one could get at us\r\nthrough this wuzzy, unless he knew the tunnel. Isn\xe2\x80\x99t this better than\r\nlyin\xe2\x80\x99 up just behind the Coll.--in a blue funk every time we had a\r\nsmoke? Isn\xe2\x80\x99t your Uncle Stalky--?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Beetle--he was stretched at the edge of the cliff spitting\r\nthoughtfully. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ve got to thank Turkey for this. Turkey is the Great\r\nMan. Turkey, dear, you\xe2\x80\x99re distressing Heffles.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGloomy old ass!\xe2\x80\x9d said McTurk, deep in a book.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99ve got us under suspicion,\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky. \xe2\x80\x9cHoophats _is_ so\r\nsuspicious somehow; and Foxy always makes every stalk he does a sort\r\nof--sort of--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cScalp,\xe2\x80\x9d said Beetle. \xe2\x80\x9cFoxy\xe2\x80\x99s a giddy Chingangook.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoor Foxy,\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s goin\xe2\x80\x99 to catch us one of these days.\r\n\xe2\x80\x99Said to me in the Gym last night, \xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99ve got my eye on you, Mister\r\nCorkran. I\xe2\x80\x99m only\r\nwarnin\xe2\x80\x99 you for your good.\xe2\x80\x99 Foxy was wrath.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but it\xe2\x80\x99s only fair sport for Foxy,\xe2\x80\x9d said Beetle. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s Hefflelinga\r\nthat has the evil mind. \xe2\x80\x99Shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t wonder if he thought we got tight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never got squiffy but once--that was in the holidays,\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky,\r\nreflectively; \xe2\x80\x9can\xe2\x80\x99 it made me horrid sick. \xe2\x80\x99Pon my sacred Sam, though,\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s enough to drive a man to drink, havin\xe2\x80\x99 an animal like Hoof for\r\nhouse-master.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf we attended the matches an\xe2\x80\x99 yelled, \xe2\x80\x98Well hit, sir,\xe2\x80\x99 an\xe2\x80\x99 stood on\r\none leg an\xe2\x80\x99 grinned every time Heffy said, \xe2\x80\x98So ho, my sons. Is it thus?\xe2\x80\x99\r\nan\xe2\x80\x99 said, \xe2\x80\x98Yes, sir,\xe2\x80\x99 an\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98No, sir,\xe2\x80\x99 an\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98O, sir,\xe2\x80\x99 an\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98Please, sir,\xe2\x80\x99\r\nlike a lot o\xe2\x80\x99 filthy fa-ags, Heffy \xe2\x80\x99ud think no end of us,\xe2\x80\x9d said McTurk\r\nwith a sneer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cToo late to begin that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s all right. The Hefflelinga means well. _But_ he is an ass. _And_\r\nwe show him that we think he\xe2\x80\x99s an ass. An\xe2\x80\x99 _so_ Heffy don\xe2\x80\x99t love us.\r\n\xe2\x80\x99Told me last night after prayers that he was _in loco parentis_,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n Beetle grunted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe deuce he did!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Stalky. \xe2\x80\x9cThat means he\xe2\x80\x99s maturin\xe2\x80\x99 something\r\nunusual dam\xe2\x80\x99 mean. Last time he told me that he gave me three hundred\r\nlines for dancin\xe2\x80\x99 the cachuca in Number Ten dormitory. _Loco parentis_,\r\nby gum! But what\xe2\x80\x99s the odds as long as you\xe2\x80\x99re \xe2\x80\x99appy? _We\xe2\x80\x99re_ all right.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey were, and their very rightness puzzled Prout, King, and the\r\nSergeant. Boys with bad consciences show it. They slink out past the\r\nFives Court in haste, and smile nervously when questioned. They return,\r\ndisordered, in bare time to save a call-over. They nod and wink and\r\ngiggle one to the other, scattering at the approach of a master. But\r\nStalky and his allies had long out-lived these manifestations of youth.\r\nThey strolled forth unconcernedly, and returned in excellent shape after\r\na light refreshment of strawberries and cream at the Lodge.\r\n\r\nThe Lodge-keeper had been promoted to keeper, _vice_ the murderous\r\nfisherman, and his wife made much of the boys. The man, too, gave them\r\na squirrel, which they presented to the Natural History Society; thereby\r\ncheckmating little Hartopp, who wished to know what they were doing for\r\nScience. Foxy faithfully worked some deep Devon lanes behind a lonely\r\ncross-roads inn; and it was curious that Prout and King, members of\r\nCommon-room seldom friendly, walked together in the same direction--that\r\nis to say, northeast.\r\n\r\nNow, the Pleasant Isle of Aves lay due southwest. \xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re\r\ndeep--day-vilish deep,\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy are they drawin\xe2\x80\x99 those\r\ncovers?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMe,\xe2\x80\x9d said Beetle sweetly. \xe2\x80\x9cI asked Foxy if he had ever tasted the beer\r\nthere. That was enough for Foxy, and it cheered him up a little. He and\r\nHeffy were sniffin\xe2\x80\x99 round our old hut so long I thought they\xe2\x80\x99d like a\r\nchange.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it can\xe2\x80\x99t last forever,\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky. \xe2\x80\x9cHeffy\xe2\x80\x99s bankin\xe2\x80\x99 up like\r\na thunder-cloud, an\xe2\x80\x99 King goes rubbin\xe2\x80\x99 his beastly hands, an\xe2\x80\x99 grinnin\xe2\x80\x99\r\nlike a hyena. He\xe2\x80\x99ll burst some\r\nday.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat day came a little sooner than they expected--came when the\r\nSergeant, whose duty it was to collect defaulters, did not attend an\r\nafternoon call-over.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTired of pubs, eh? Come\r\non!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAves?\xe2\x80\x9d said Beetle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course, but I\xe2\x80\x99m not smokin\xe2\x80\x99 _aujourd\xe2\x80\x99hui_. _Parceque je_ jolly well\r\n_pense_ that we\xe2\x80\x99ll be _suivi_. We\xe2\x80\x99ll go along the cliffs, slow, an\xe2\x80\x99 give\r\nFoxy lots of time to parallel us up above.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey strolled towards the swimming-baths, and presently overtook King.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, don\xe2\x80\x99t let _me_ interrupt you,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cEngaged in scientific\r\npursuits, of course? I trust you will enjoy yourselves, my young\r\nfriends.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou see!\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky, when they were out of earshot. \xe2\x80\x9cHe _can\xe2\x80\x99t_ keep\r\na secret. He\xe2\x80\x99s followin\xe2\x80\x99 to cut off our line of retreat. He\xe2\x80\x99ll wait\r\nat the baths till Heffy comes along. They\xe2\x80\x99ve tried every blessed place\r\nexcept along the cliffs, and now they think they\xe2\x80\x99ve bottled us. No need\r\nto hurry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey walked leisurely over the combes till they reached the line of\r\nnotice-boards.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cListen a shake. Foxy\xe2\x80\x99s up wind comin\xe2\x80\x99 down hill like beans. When you\r\nhear him move in the bushes, go straight across to Aves. They want to\r\ncatch us _flagrante delicto_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey dived into the gorse at right angles to the tunnel, openly crossing\r\nthe grass, and lay still in Aves.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did I tell you?\xe2\x80\x9d Stalky carefully put away the pipes and tobacco.\r\nThe Sergeant, out of breath, was leaning against the fence, raking\r\nthe furze with his binoculars, but he might as well have tried to see\r\nthrough a sand-bag. Anon, Prout and King appeared behind him. They\r\nconferred.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAha! Foxy don\xe2\x80\x99t like the notice-boards, and he don\xe2\x80\x99t like the prickles\r\neither. Now we\xe2\x80\x99ll cut up the tunnel and go to the Lodge. Hullo! They\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nsent Foxy into cover.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Sergeant was waist-deep in crackling, swaying furze, his ears filled\r\nwith the noise of his own progress. The boys reached the shelter of the\r\nwood and looked down through a belt of hollies.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHellish noise!\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky, critically. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Don\xe2\x80\x99t think Colonel Dabney\r\nwill like it. I move we go into the Lodge and get something to eat. We\r\nmight as well see the fun out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSuddenly the keeper passed them at a trot. \xe2\x80\x9cWho\xe2\x80\x99m they to combe-bottom\r\nfor Lard\xe2\x80\x99s sake? Master\xe2\x80\x99ll be crazy,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoachers simly,\xe2\x80\x9d Stalky replied in the broad Devon that was the boy\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n_langue de guerre_.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll poach \xe2\x80\x99em to raights!\xe2\x80\x9d He dropped into the funnel-like combe,\r\nwhich presently began to fill with noises, notably King\xe2\x80\x99s voice crying:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo on, Sergeant! Leave him alone, you, sir. He is executing my orders.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho\xe2\x80\x99m yeou to give arders here, gingy whiskers? Yeou come up to the\r\nmaster. Come out o\xe2\x80\x99 that wuzzy! [This is to the Sergeant.] Yiss, I\r\nreckon us knows the boys yeou\xe2\x80\x99m after. They\xe2\x80\x99ve tu long ears an\xe2\x80\x99 vuzzy\r\nbellies, an\xe2\x80\x99 you nippies they in yeour pockets when they\xe2\x80\x99m dead. Come on\r\nup to master! He\xe2\x80\x99ll boy yeou all you\xe2\x80\x99re a mind to. Yeou other folk bide\r\nyour side fence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExplain to the proprietor. You can explain, Sergeant,\xe2\x80\x9d shouted King.\r\nEvidently the Sergeant had surrendered to the major force.\r\n\r\nBeetle lay at full length on the turf behind the Lodge, literally biting\r\nthe earth in spasms of joy. Stalky kicked him upright. There was nothing\r\nof levity about Stalky or McTurk save a stray muscle twitching on the\r\ncheek.\r\n\r\nThey tapped at the Lodge door, where they were always welcome. \xe2\x80\x9cCome\r\nyeou right in an\xe2\x80\x99 set down, my little dearrs,\xe2\x80\x9d said the woman. \xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nniver touch my man. He\xe2\x80\x99ll poach \xe2\x80\x99em to rights. Iss fai! Fresh berries\r\nan\xe2\x80\x99 cream. Us Dartymoor folk niver forgit their friends. But them\r\nBidevor poachers, they\xe2\x80\x99ve no hem to their garments. Sugar? My man he\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\ndigged a badger for yeou, my dearrs. \xe2\x80\x99Tis in the linhay in a box.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUs\xe2\x80\x99ll take un with us when we\xe2\x80\x99re finished here. I reckon yeou\xe2\x80\x99m busy.\r\nWe\xe2\x80\x99ll bide here an\xe2\x80\x99--\xe2\x80\x99tis washin\xe2\x80\x99 day with yeou, simly,\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99m no company to make all vitty for. Never yeou mind us. Yiss.\r\nThere\xe2\x80\x99s plenty cream.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe woman withdrew, wiping her pink hands on her apron, and left them\r\nin the parlor. There was a scuffle of feet on the gravel outside the\r\nheavily-leaded diamond panes, and then the voice of Colonel Dabney,\r\nsomething clearer than a bugle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYe can read? You\xe2\x80\x99ve eyes in your head? Don\xe2\x80\x99t attempt to deny it. Ye\r\nhave!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBeetle snatched a crochet-work antimacassar from the shiny horsehair\r\nsofa, stuffed it into his mouth, and rolled out of sight.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou saw my notice-boards. Your duty? Curse your impudence, sir. Your\r\nduty was to keep off my grounds. Talk of duty to _me_! Why--why--why, ye\r\nmisbegotten poacher, ye\xe2\x80\x99ll be teaching me my A B C next! Roarin\xe2\x80\x99 like a\r\nbull in the bushes down there! Boys? Boys? Boys? Keep your boys at home,\r\nthen! I\xe2\x80\x99m not responsible for your boys! But I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe it--I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t believe a word of it. Ye\xe2\x80\x99ve a furtive look in your eye--a furtive,\r\nsneakin\xe2\x80\x99, poachin\xe2\x80\x99 look in your eye, that \xe2\x80\x99ud ruin the reputation of an\r\narchangel! Don\xe2\x80\x99t attempt to deny it! Ye have! A sergeant? More shame to\r\nyou, then, an\xe2\x80\x99 the worst bargain Her Majesty ever made! A sergeant, to\r\nrun about the country poachin\xe2\x80\x99--on your pension! Damnable! Oh, damnable!\r\nBut I\xe2\x80\x99ll be considerate. I\xe2\x80\x99ll be merciful. By gad, I\xe2\x80\x99ll be the very\r\nessence o\xe2\x80\x99 humanity! Did ye, or did ye not, see my notice-boards? Don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nattempt to deny it! Ye did. Silence, Sergeant!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTwenty-one years in the army had left their mark on Foxy. He obeyed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow. March!\xe2\x80\x9d The high Lodge gate shut with a clang. \xe2\x80\x9cMy duty! A\r\nsergeant to tell me my duty!\xe2\x80\x9d puffed Colonel Dabney. \xe2\x80\x9cGood Lard! more\r\nsergeants!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s King! It\xe2\x80\x99s King!\xe2\x80\x9d gulped Stalky, his head on the horsehair pillow.\r\nMcTurk was eating the rag-carpet before the speckless hearth, and the\r\nsofa heaved to the emotions of Beetle. Through the thick glass the\r\nfigures without showed blue, distorted, and menacing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI--I protest against this outrage.\xe2\x80\x9d King had evidently been running\r\nup hill. \xe2\x80\x9cThe man was entirely within his duty. Let--let me give you my\r\ncard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s in flannels!\xe2\x80\x9d Stalky buried his head again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUnfortunately--_most_ unfortunately--I have not one with me, but my\r\nname is King, sir, a house-master of the College, and you will find me\r\nprepared--fully prepared--to answer for this man\xe2\x80\x99s action. We\xe2\x80\x99ve seen\r\nthree--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Did_ ye see my notice-boards?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI admit we did; but under the circumstances--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI stand _in loco parentis_.\xe2\x80\x9d Prout\xe2\x80\x99s deep voice was added to the\r\ndiscussion. They could hear him pant.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cF\xe2\x80\x99what?\xe2\x80\x9d Colonel Dabney was growing more and more Irish.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m responsible for the boys under my charge.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYe are, are ye? Ye did. Well,\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ve not forgotten my Latin either, an\xe2\x80\x99 I\xe2\x80\x99ll say to you:\r\n\xe2\x80\x98_Quis custodiet ipsos custodes_.\xe2\x80\x99 If the masters trespass, how can we\r\nblame the boys?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut if I could speak to you privately,\xe2\x80\x9d said Prout.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll have nothing private with you! Ye can be as private as ye please\r\non the other side o\xe2\x80\x99 that gate an\xe2\x80\x99--I wish ye a very good afternoon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA second time the gate clanged. They waited till Colonel Dabney had\r\nreturned to the house, and fell into one another\xe2\x80\x99s arms, crowing for\r\nbreath.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, my Soul! Oh, my King! Oh, my Heffy! Oh, my Foxy! Zeal, all zeal,\r\nMr. Simple.\xe2\x80\x9d Stalky wiped his eyes. \xe2\x80\x9cOh! Oh! Oh!--\xe2\x80\x98I _did_ boil the\r\nexciseman!\xe2\x80\x99 We must get out of this or we\xe2\x80\x99ll be late for tea.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGe--Ge--get the badger and make little Hartopp happy. Ma--ma--make\r\n\xe2\x80\x99em all happy,\xe2\x80\x9d sobbed McTurk, groping for the door and kicking the\r\nprostrate Beetle before him.\r\n\r\nThey found the beast in an evil-smelling box, left two half-crowns for\r\npayment, and staggered home. Only the badger grunted most marvelous like\r\nColonel Dabney, and they dropped him twice or thrice with shrieks of\r\nhelpless laughter. They were but imperfectly recovered when Foxy met\r\nthem by the Fives Court with word that they were to go up to their\r\ndormitory and wait till sent for.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, take this box to Mr. Hartopp\xe2\x80\x99s rooms, then. We\xe2\x80\x99ve done something\r\nfor the Natural History Society, at any rate,\xe2\x80\x9d said Beetle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Fraid that won\xe2\x80\x99t save you, young gen\xe2\x80\x99elmen,\xe2\x80\x9d Foxy answered, in an\r\nawful voice. He was sorely ruffled in his mind.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll sereno, Foxibus.\xe2\x80\x9d Stalky had reached the extreme stage of hiccups.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe--we\xe2\x80\x99ll never desert you, Foxy. Hounds choppin\xe2\x80\x99 foxes in cover is\r\nmore a proof of vice, ain\xe2\x80\x99t it?... No, you\xe2\x80\x99re right. I\xe2\x80\x99m--I\xe2\x80\x99m not quite\r\nwell.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99ve gone a bit too far this time,\xe2\x80\x9d Foxy thought to himself. \xe2\x80\x9cVery\r\nfar gone, _I\xe2\x80\x99d_ say, excep\xe2\x80\x99 there was no smell of liquor. An\xe2\x80\x99 yet it\r\nisn\xe2\x80\x99t like \xe2\x80\x99em--somehow. King and Prout they \xe2\x80\x99ad their dressin\xe2\x80\x99-down\r\nsame as me. That\xe2\x80\x99s one comfort.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, we must pull up,\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky, rising from the bed on which he had\r\nthrown himself. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re injured innocence--as usual. _We_ don\xe2\x80\x99t know what\r\nwe\xe2\x80\x99ve been sent up here for, do we?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo explanation. Deprived of tea. Public disgrace before the house,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said McTurk, whose eyes were running over. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s dam\xe2\x80\x99 serious.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, hold on, till King loses his temper,\xe2\x80\x9d said Beetle. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\nlibelous old rip, an\xe2\x80\x99 he\xe2\x80\x99ll be in a ravin\xe2\x80\x99 paddy-wack. Prout\xe2\x80\x99s too\r\nbeastly cautious. Keep your eye on King, and, if he gives us a chance,\r\nappeal to the Head. That always makes \xe2\x80\x99em sick.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey were summoned to their house-master\xe2\x80\x99s study, King and Foxy\r\nsupporting Prout, and Foxy had three canes under his arm. King leered\r\ntriumphantly, for there were tears, undried tears of mirth, on the boys\xe2\x80\x99\r\ncheeks. Then the examination began.\r\n\r\nYes, they had walked along the cliffs. Yes, they had entered Colonel\r\nDabney\xe2\x80\x99s grounds. Yes, they had seen the notice-boards (at this point\r\nBeetle sputtered hysterically). For what purpose had they entered\r\nColonel Dabney\xe2\x80\x99s grounds? \xe2\x80\x9cWell, sir, there was a badger.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHere King, who loathed the Natural History Society because he did not\r\nlike Hartopp, could no longer be restrained. He begged them not to add\r\nmendacity to open insolence. But the badger was in Mr. Hartopp\xe2\x80\x99s rooms,\r\nsir. The Sergeant had kindly taken it up for them. That disposed of the\r\nbadger, and the temporary check brought King\xe2\x80\x99s temper to boiling-point.\r\nThey could hear his foot on the floor while Prout prepared his lumbering\r\ninquiries. They had settled into their stride now. Their eyes ceased to\r\nsparkle; their faces were blank; their hands hung beside them without a\r\ntwitch. They were learning, at the expense of a fellow-countryman, the\r\nlesson of their race, which is to put away all emotion and entrap the\r\nalien at the proper time.\r\n\r\nSo far good. King was importing himself more freely into the trial,\r\nbeing vengeful where Prout was grieved. They knew the penalties of\r\ntrespassing? With a fine show of irresolution, Stalky admitted that\r\nhe had gathered some information vaguely bearing on this head, but he\r\nthought--The sentence was dragged out to the uttermost: Stalky did not\r\nwish to play his trump with such an opponent. Mr. King desired no buts,\r\nnor was he interested in Stalky\xe2\x80\x99s evasions. They, on the other hand,\r\nmight be interested in his poor views. Boys who crept--who sneaked--who\r\nlurked--out of bounds, even the generous bounds of the Natural\r\nHistory Society, which they had falsely joined as a cloak for their\r\nmisdeeds--their vices--their villainies--their immoralities--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99ll break cover in a minute,\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky to himself. \xe2\x80\x9cThen we\xe2\x80\x99ll run\r\ninto him before he gets away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSuch boys, scabrous boys, moral lepers--the current of his words was\r\ncarrying King off his feet--evil-speakers, liars, slow-bellies--yea,\r\nincipient drunkards...\r\n\r\nHe was merely working up to a peroration, and the boys knew it; but\r\nMcTurk cut through the frothing sentence, the others echoing:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI appeal to the Head, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI appeal to the head, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI appeal to the Head, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was their unquestioned right. Drunkenness meant expulsion after a\r\npublic flogging. They had been accused of it. The case was the Head\xe2\x80\x99s,\r\nand the Head\xe2\x80\x99s alone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou hast appealed unto Caesar: unto Caesar shalt thou go.\xe2\x80\x9d They had\r\nheard that sentence once or twice before in their careers. \xe2\x80\x9cNone the\r\nless,\xe2\x80\x9d said King, uneasily, \xe2\x80\x9cyou would be better advised to abide by our\r\ndecision, my young friends.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre we allowed to associate with the rest of the school till we see the\r\nHead, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d said McTurk to his house-master, disregarding King. This at\r\nonce lifted the situation to its loftiest plane. Moreover, it meant no\r\nwork, for moral leprosy was strictly quarantined, and the Head never\r\nexecuted judgment till twenty-four cold hours later.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell--er--if you persist in your defiant attitude,\xe2\x80\x9d said King, with a\r\nloving look at the canes under Foxy\xe2\x80\x99s arm. \xe2\x80\x9cThere is no alternative.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTen minutes later the news was over the whole school. Stalky and Co.\r\nhad fallen at last--fallen by drink. They had been drinking. They had\r\nreturned blind-drunk from a hut. They were even now lying hopelessly\r\nintoxicated on the dormitory floor. A few bold spirits crept up to look,\r\nand received boots about the head from the criminals.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ve got him--got him on the Caudine Toasting-fork!\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky,\r\nafter those hints were taken. \xe2\x80\x9cKing\xe2\x80\x99ll have to prove his charges up to\r\nthe giddy hilt.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cToo much ticklee, him bust,\xe2\x80\x9d Beetle quoted from a book of his reading.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDidn\xe2\x80\x99t I say he\xe2\x80\x99d go pop if we lat un bide?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo prep., either, O ye incipient drunkards,\xe2\x80\x9d said McTurk, \xe2\x80\x9cand it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ntrig night, too. Hullo! Here\xe2\x80\x99s our dear friend Foxy. More tortures,\r\nFoxibus?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve brought you something to eat, young gentlemen,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Sergeant\r\nfrom behind a crowded tray. Their wars had ever been waged without\r\nmalice, and a suspicion floated in Foxy\xe2\x80\x99s mind that boys who allowed\r\nthemselves to be tracked so easily might, perhaps, hold something in\r\nreserve. Foxy had served through the Mutiny, when early and accurate\r\ninformation was worth much.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI--I noticed you \xe2\x80\x99adn\xe2\x80\x99t \xe2\x80\x99ad anything to eat, an\xe2\x80\x99 I spoke to Gumbly, an\xe2\x80\x99\r\nhe said you wasn\xe2\x80\x99t exactly cut off from supplies. That\xe2\x80\x99s young\r\nCarter\xe2\x80\x99s potted ham, though.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere was a C on it. I thought it was Mr. Corkran\xe2\x80\x99s. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know,\r\nperhaps, but there might be something on your side which you hadn\xe2\x80\x99t said\r\nto Mr. King or Mr. Prout, maybe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is. Heaps, Foxibus.\xe2\x80\x9d This from Stalky through a full mouth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you see, if that was the case, it seemed to me I might represent\r\nit, quiet so to say, to the \xe2\x80\x99Ead when he asks me about it. I\xe2\x80\x99ve got to\r\ntake \xe2\x80\x99im the charges to-night, an\xe2\x80\x99--it looks bad on the face of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Trocious bad, Foxy. Twenty-seven cuts in the Gym before all the\r\nschool, and public expulsion. \xe2\x80\x98Wine is a mocker, strong drink is\r\nragin\xe2\x80\x99,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d quoth Beetle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s nothin\xe2\x80\x99 to make fun of, young gentlemen. Hurry\r\nup, Turkey.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMcTurk, in a violet silk skirt and a coquettish blue turban, slouched\r\nforward as one thoroughly ashamed of himself. It happened to be King, in gown and mortar-board,\r\nenjoying a Saturday evening prowl before dinner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLocked doors! Locked doors!\xe2\x80\x9d he snapped with a scowl. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the\r\nmeaning of this; and what, may I ask, is the intention of this--this\r\nepicene attire?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPantomime, sir. A gray princess-skirt borrowed from a day-boy\xe2\x80\x99s mother and a\r\nspotted cotton bodice unsystematically padded with imposition-paper make\r\none ridiculous. And in other regards Beetle had a bad conscience.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs usual!\xe2\x80\x9d sneered King. \xe2\x80\x9cFutile foolery just when your careers, such\r\nas they may be, are hanging in the balance. I see! Ah, I see! The old\r\ngang of criminals--allied forces of disorder--Corkran\xe2\x80\x9d--the Slave of the\r\nLamp smiled politely--\xe2\x80\x9cMcTurk\xe2\x80\x9d--the Irishman scowled--\xe2\x80\x9cand, of course,\r\nthe unspeakable Beetle, our friend Gigadibs.\xe2\x80\x9d Abanazar, the Emperor, and\r\nAladdin had more or less of characters, and King passed them over. \xe2\x80\x9cCome\r\nforth, my inky buffoon, from behind yonder instrument of music! You\r\nsupply, I presume, the doggerel for this entertainment. Esteem yourself\r\nto be, as it were, a poet?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s found one of \xe2\x80\x99em,\xe2\x80\x9d thought Beetle, noting the flush on King\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncheek-bone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have just had the pleasure of reading an effusion of yours to\r\nmy address, I believe--an effusion intended to rhyme. So--so you\r\ndespise me, Master Gigadibs, do you? I am quite aware--you need not\r\nexplain--that it was ostensibly not intended for my edification. I\r\nread it with laughter--yes, with laughter. These paper pellets of\r\ninky boys--still a boy we are, Master Gigadibs--do not disturb my\r\nequanimity.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWonder which it was,\xe2\x80\x9d thought Beetle. He had launched many lampoons on\r\nan appreciative public ever since he discovered that it was possible to\r\nconvey reproof in rhyme.\r\n\r\nIn sign of his unruffled calm, King proceeded to tear Beetle, whom he\r\ncalled Gigadibs, slowly asunder. From his untied shoestrings to his\r\nmended spectacles (the life of a poet at a big school is hard) he held\r\nhim up to the derision of his associates--with the usual result. His\r\nwild flowers of speech--King had an unpleasant tongue---restored him to\r\ngood humor at the last. He drew a lurid picture of Beetle\xe2\x80\x99s latter\r\nend as a scurrilous pamphleteer dying in an attic, scattered a few\r\ncompliments over McTurk and Corkran, and, reminding Beetle that he must\r\ncome up for judgment when called upon, went to Common-room, where he\r\ntriumphed anew over his victims.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the worst of it,\xe2\x80\x9d he explained in a loud voice over his soup, \xe2\x80\x9cis\r\nthat I waste such gems of sarcasm on their thick heads. Number Five study had a reputation for\r\nmore variegated insanity than the rest of the school put together; and\r\nso far as its code allowed friendship with outsiders it was polite and\r\nopen-hearted to its neighbors on the same landing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat rot do you want now?\xe2\x80\x9d said Beetle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKing! The blow fell without warning. So it was left to McTurk to sum up the\r\nsituation beneath the guns of the enemy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou see,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Irishman, hanging on the banister, \xe2\x80\x9che begins by\r\nbullying little chaps; then he bullies the big chaps; then he bullies\r\nsome one who isn\xe2\x80\x99t connected with the College, and then catches it.\r\nServes him jolly well right... As they\r\nwere grievously in arrears with a Latin prose, as it was a blazing July\r\nafternoon, and as they ought to have been at a house cricket-match,\r\nthey began to renew their acquaintance, intimate and unholy, with the\r\nvolumes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere we are!\xe2\x80\x9d said McTurk. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Corporal punishment produced on Eric the\r\nworst effects. He had tried the\r\nexperiment once at a big match, when the three, self-isolated, stood\r\nto attention for half an hour in full view of all the visitors, to whom\r\nfags, subsidized for that end, pointed them out as victims of Prout\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ntyranny. And Prout was a sensitive man.\r\n\r\nIn the infinitely petty confederacies of the Common-room, King and\r\nMacrea, fellow house-masters, had borne it in upon him that by games,\r\nand games alone, was salvation wrought. Boys neglected were boys lost.\r\nThey must be disciplined. Left to himself, Prout would have made\r\na sympathetic house-master; but he was never so left, and with the\r\ndevilish insight of youth, the boys knew to whom they were indebted for\r\nhis zeal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMust we go down, sir?\xe2\x80\x99 said McTurk.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want to order you to do what a right-thinking boy should do\r\ngladly. What were their\r\nviews concerning the government of the school, that he might hasten\r\nto give effect to them? he would not offend them for worlds; but\r\nhe feared--he sadly feared--that his own house, who did not pass\r\nresolutions (but washed), might somewhat deride.\r\n\r\nKing was a happy man, and his house, basking in the favor of his smile,\r\nmade that afternoon a long penance to the misled Prouts. Naturally they take\r\nadvantage of what to them is a heaven-sent opportunity,\xe2\x80\x9d said little\r\nHartopp. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat _is_ the trouble in your dormitories, King?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. King explained that as he had made it the one rule of his life never\r\nto interfere with another man\xe2\x80\x99s house, so he expected not to be too\r\npatently interfered with. They might be interested to learn--here the\r\nchaplain heaved a weary sigh--that he had taken all steps that, in his\r\npoor judgment, would meet the needs of the case. Nay, further, he had\r\nhimself expended, with no thought of reimbursement, sums, the amount of\r\nwhich he would not specify, on disinfectants. This he had done because\r\nhe knew by bitter--by most bitter--experience that the management of the\r\ncollege was slack, dilatory, and inefficient. He might even add, almost\r\nas slack as the administration of certain houses which now thought\r\nfit to sit in judgment on his actions. With a short summary of his\r\nscholastic career, and a precis of his qualifications, including his\r\ndegrees, he withdrew, slamming the door.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHeigho!\xe2\x80\x9d said the chaplain. \xe2\x80\x9cOurs is a dwarfing life--a belittling\r\nlife, my brethren. God help all schoolmasters! They need it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t like the boys, I own\xe2\x80\x9d--Prout dug viciously with his fork into\r\nthe table-cloth--\xe2\x80\x9cand I don\xe2\x80\x99t pretend to be a strong man, as you know.\r\nBut I confess I can\xe2\x80\x99t see any reason why I should take steps against\r\nStalky and the others because King happens to be annoyed by--by--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFalling into the pit he has digged,\xe2\x80\x9d said little Hartopp. \xe2\x80\x9cCertainly\r\nnot, Prout. John Gillett\xe2\x80\x99s good generalship. Since\r\nthe discovery of the cat, King had been too ready to see affront\r\nwhere none was meant, and the Reverend John, buffer-state and general\r\nconfidant, had worked for a week to bring about a good understanding.\r\nHe was fat, clean-shaven, except for a big mustache, of an imperturbable\r\ngood temper, and, those who loved him least said, a guileful Jesuit.\r\nHe smiled benignantly upon his handiwork--four sorely tried men talking\r\nwithout very much malice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow remember,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, when the conversation turned that way, \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nimpute nothing. But every time that any one has taken direct steps\r\nagainst Number Five study, the issue has been more or less humiliating\r\nto the taker.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t admit that. I pulverize the egregious Beetle daily for his\r\nsoul\xe2\x80\x99s good; and the others with him,\xe2\x80\x9d said King.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, take your own case, King, and go back a couple of years. Do\r\nyou remember when Prout and you were on their track for--hutting and\r\ntrespass, wasn\xe2\x80\x99t it? Have you forgotten Colonel Dabney?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe others laughed. King did not care to be reminded of his career as a\r\npoacher.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was one instance. That same evening your study was\r\nwrecked.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy Rabbits-Eggs--most beastly drunk--from the road,\xe2\x80\x9d said King. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\r\nhas that--?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Reverend John went on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLastly, they conceive that aspersions are cast upon their personal\r\ncleanliness--a most delicate matter with all boys. Ve-ry good. Observe\r\nhow, in each case, the punishment fits the crime. A week after your\r\nhouse calls them \xe2\x80\x98stinkers,\xe2\x80\x99 King, your house is, not to put too fine a\r\npoint on it, stunk out by a dead cat who chooses to die in the one\r\nspot where she can annoy you most. Again the long arm of coincidence!\r\n_Summa_. You accuse them of trespass. Through some absurd chain of\r\ncircumstances--they may or may not be at the other end of it--you and\r\nProut are made to appear as trespassers. You evict them. For a time your\r\nstudy is made untenable. I have drawn the parallel in the last case.\r\nWell?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe was under the centre of White\xe2\x80\x99s dormitory,\xe2\x80\x9d said King. \xe2\x80\x9cThere are\r\ndouble floor-boards there to deaden noise. No boy, even in my own house,\r\ncould possibly have pried up the boards without leaving some trace--and\r\nRabbits-Eggs was phenomenally drunk that other night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are singularly favored by fortune. That is all I ever said.\r\nPersonally, I like them immensely, and I believe I have a little of\r\ntheir confidence. I confess I like being called \xe2\x80\x98Padre.\xe2\x80\x99 They are at\r\npeace with me; consequently I am not treated to bogus confessions of\r\ntheft.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mean Mason\xe2\x80\x99s case?\xe2\x80\x9d said Prout heavily. \xe2\x80\x9cThat always struck me\r\nas peculiarly scandalous. I thought the Head should have taken up the\r\nmatter more thoroughly. Mason may be misguided, but at least he is\r\nthoroughly sincere and means well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI confess I cannot agree with you, Prout,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Reverend John. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\r\njumped at some silly tale of theft on their part; accepted another boy\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nevidence without, so far as I can see, any inquiry; and--frankly, I\r\nthink he deserved all he got.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey deliberately outraged Mason\xe2\x80\x99s best feelings,\xe2\x80\x9d said Prout. \xe2\x80\x9cA word\r\nto me on their part would have saved the whole thing. But they preferred\r\nto lure him on; to play on his ignorance of their characters--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat may be,\xe2\x80\x9d said King, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I don\xe2\x80\x99t like Mason. He means well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOur criminal tradition is not theft--among ourselves, at least,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nlittle Hartopp.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor the head of a house that raided seven head of cattle from the\r\ninnocent pot-wallopers of Northam, isn\xe2\x80\x99t that rather a sweeping\r\nstatement?\xe2\x80\x9d said Macrea.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPrecisely so,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hartopp, unabashed. \xe2\x80\x9cThat, with gate-lifting, and a\r\nlittle poaching and hawk-hunting on the cliffs, is our salvation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt does us far more harm as a school--\xe2\x80\x9d Prout began.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThan any hushed-up scandal could? Quite so. Our reputation among the\r\nfarmers is most unsavory. But I would much sooner deal with any amount\r\nof ingenious crime of that nature than--some other offenses.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey may be all right, but they are unboylike, abnormal, and, in\r\nmy opinion, unsound,\xe2\x80\x9d Prout insisted. \xe2\x80\x9cThe moral effect of their\r\nperformances must pave the way for greater harm. I might separate them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou might, of course; but they have gone up the school together for six\r\nyears. _I_ shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t care to do it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Macrea.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey use the editorial \xe2\x80\x98we,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d said King, irrelevantly. \xe2\x80\x9cIt annoys me.\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Where\xe2\x80\x99s your prose, Corkran?\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98Well, sir, we haven\xe2\x80\x99t quite done it\r\nyet.\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98We\xe2\x80\x99ll bring it in a minute,\xe2\x80\x99 and so on. And the same with the\r\nothers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s great virtue in that \xe2\x80\x98we,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d said little Hartopp. \xe2\x80\x9cYou know I\r\ntake them for trig. McTurk may have some conception of the meaning of\r\nit; but Beetle is as the brutes that perish about sines and cosines. He\r\ncopies serenely from Stalky, who positively rejoices in mathematics.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy don\xe2\x80\x99t you stop it?\xe2\x80\x9d said Prout.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt rights itself at the exams. Where are you going?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNonsense! They would not dare---but I am going to think this out,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nProut. \xe2\x80\x9cIt needs thought. Mark my words,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nReverend John. \xe2\x80\x9cThere will be trouble.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n       *       *       *       *       *\r\n\r\n   But by the yellow Tiber\r\n   Was tumult and affright.\r\n\r\nOut of the blue sky (they were still rejoicing over the cat war) Mr.\r\nProut had dropped into Number Five, read them a lecture on the enormity\r\nof cribbing, and bidden them return to the form-rooms on Monday. They\r\nhad raged, solo and chorus, all through the peaceful Sabbath, for their\r\nsin was more or less the daily practice of all the studies.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the good of cursing?\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky at last. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re all in the\r\nsame boat. A locker\r\nin the form-room, and a seat at prep. in Number Twelve.\xe2\x80\x9d (He looked\r\nregretfully round the cozy study which McTurk, their leader in matters\r\nof Art, had decorated with a dado, a stencil, and cretonne hangings.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes! Come on!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe house received them on their fall with the mixture of jest and\r\nsympathy always extended to boys turned out of their study. The known\r\naloofness of the three made them more interesting.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite like old times, ain\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d Stalky selected a locker and flung in\r\nhis books. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ve come to sport with you, my young friends, for a while,\r\nbecause our beloved house-master has hove us out of our diggin\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Serve you jolly well right,\xe2\x80\x9d said Orrin, \xe2\x80\x9cyou cribbers!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis will never do,\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky. \xe2\x80\x9cWe can\xe2\x80\x99t maintain our giddy\r\nprestige, Orrin, de-ah, if you make these remarks.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey wrapped themselves lovingly about the boy, thrust him to the opened\r\nwindow, and drew down the sash to the nape of his neck. With an equal\r\nswiftness they tied his thumbs together behind his back with a piece of\r\ntwine, and then, because he kicked furiously, removed his shoes. There\r\nMr. Prout happened to find him a few minutes later, guillotined and\r\nhelpless, surrounded by a convulsed crowd who would not assist.\r\n\r\nStalky, in an upper form-room, had gathered himself allies against\r\nvengeance. Orrin presently tore up at the head of a boarding party, and\r\nthe form-room grew one fog of dust through which boys wrestled, stamped,\r\nshouted, and yelled. A desk was carried away in the tumult, a knot of\r\nwarriors reeled into and split a door-panel, a window was broken, and\r\na gas-jet fell. Under cover of the confusion the three escaped to the\r\ncorridor, whence they called in and sent up passers-by to the fray.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRescue, Kings! Kings! Kings! Number Twelve form-room! Rescue, Hartopps!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe juniors hurried out like bees aswarm, asking no questions, clattered\r\nup the staircase, and added themselves to the embroilment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot bad for the first evening\xe2\x80\x99s work,\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky, rearranging his\r\ncollar. \xe2\x80\x9cI fancy Prout\xe2\x80\x99ll be somewhat annoyed. You must be beastly rich, Beetle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Beetle lent me sixpence.\xe2\x80\x9d Stalky came to a full stop and made as\r\nto work it out on his fingers. \xe2\x80\x9cSixpence on the nineteenth, didn\xe2\x80\x99t he?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; but you\xe2\x80\x99ve forgotten you paid no interest on the other bob--the\r\none I lent you before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you took my watch as security.\xe2\x80\x9d The game was developing itself\r\nalmost automatically.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind. In the hopelessly involved finances of the study there\r\nwas just that sum to which both McTurk and Beetle laid claim, as their\r\nshare in the pledging of Stalky\xe2\x80\x99s second-best Sunday trousers. But\r\nStalky had maintained for two terms that the money was his \xe2\x80\x9ccommission\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n for effecting the pawn; and had, of course, spent it on a study \xe2\x80\x9cbrew.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUnderstand this, then. Two and fourpence, you said, Corkran?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nStalky had said nothing, and continued so to do.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour influence for evil is quite strong enough without buying a hold\r\nover your companions.\xe2\x80\x9d He felt in his pockets, and (oh joy!) produced\r\na florin and fourpence. \xe2\x80\x9cBring me what you call Corkran\xe2\x80\x99s note-of-hand,\r\nand be thankful that I do not carry the matter any further. The money\r\nis stopped from your pocket-money, Corkran. The receipt to my study, at\r\nonce!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLittle they cared! Two and fourpence in a lump is worth six weekly\r\nsixpences any hungry day of the week.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what the dooce _is_ a note-of-hand?\xe2\x80\x9d said Beetle. \xe2\x80\x9cI only read\r\nabout it in a book.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow you\xe2\x80\x99ve jolly well got to make one,\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes--but our ink don\xe2\x80\x99t turn black till next day. Stalky\xe2\x80\x99d never have\r\npaid... Why, you ass!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMechanically Beetle had handed over the money to Stalky as treasurer of\r\nthe study. The custom of years is not lightly broken. In return for\r\nthe document, Prout expounded to Beetle the enormity of money-lending,\r\nwhich, like everything except compulsory cricket, corrupted houses and\r\ndestroyed good feeling among boys, made youth cold and calculating,\r\nand opened the door to all evil. Finally, did Beetle know of any\r\nother cases? If so, it was his duty as proof of repentance to let his\r\nhouse-master know. No names need be mentioned.\r\n\r\nBeetle did not know--at least, he was not quite sure, sir. How could he\r\ngive evidence against his friends? The house might, of course--here he\r\nfeigned an anguished delicacy--be full of it. He was not in a position\r\nto say. They\xe2\x80\x99re so beastly plausible.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd Mr. Prout called the good boys into his study anew, and succeeded\r\nin sinking both his and their innocent minds ten fathoms deeper in\r\nblindfolded bedazement. He spoke of steps and measures, of tone and\r\nloyalty in the house and to the house, and urged them to take up the\r\nmatter tactfully.\r\n\r\nSo they demanded of Beetle whether he had any connection with any other\r\nestablishment. Beetle promptly went to his house-master, and wished\r\nto know by what right Harrison and Craye had reopened a matter already\r\nsettled between him and his house-master. In injured innocence no boy\r\nexcelled Beetle.\r\n\r\nThen it occurred to Prout that he might have been unfair to the culprit,\r\nwho had not striven to deny or palliate his offense. He sent for\r\nHarrison and Craye, reprehending them very gently for the tone they had\r\nadopted to a repentant sinner, and when they returned to their study,\r\nthey used the language of despair. They then made headlong inquisition\r\nthrough the house, driving the fags to the edge of hysterics, and\r\nunearthing, with tremendous pomp and parade, the natural and inevitable\r\nsystem of small loans that prevails among small boys.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou see, Harrison, Thornton minor lent me a penny last Saturday,\r\nbecause I was fined for breaking the window; and I spent it at Keyte\xe2\x80\x99s.\r\nI didn\xe2\x80\x99t know there was any harm in it.
