We\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t know there was anything wrong in it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey waded through hours of this kind of thing, but found no usury,\r\nor anything approaching to Beetle\xe2\x80\x99s gorgeous scale of interest. The\r\nseniors--for the school had no tradition of deference to prefects\r\noutside compulsory games--told them succinctly to go about their\r\nbusiness. They would not give evidence on any terms. Harrison was one\r\nidiot, and Craye was another; but the greatest of all, they said, was\r\ntheir house-master.\r\n\r\nWhen a house is thoroughly upset, however good its conscience, it breaks\r\ninto knots and coteries--small gatherings in the twilight, box-room\r\ncommittees, and groups in the corridor. And when from group to group,\r\nwith an immense affectation of secrecy, three wicked boys steal, crying\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Cav\xc3\xa9_\xe2\x80\x9d when there is no need of caution, and whispering \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t tell!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n on the heels of trumpery confidences that instant invented, a very fine\r\nair of plot and intrigue can be woven round such a house.\r\n\r\nAt the end of a few days, it dawned on Prout that he moved in an\r\natmosphere of perpetual ambush. Mysteries hedged him on all sides,\r\nwarnings ran before his heavy feet, and countersigns were muttered\r\nbehind his attentive back. McTurk and Stalky invented many absurd and\r\nidle phrases--catch-words that swept through the house as fire through\r\nstubble. It was a rare jest, and the only practical outcome of the Usury\r\nCommission, that one boy should say to a friend, with awful gravity,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think there\xe2\x80\x99s much of it going on in the house?\xe2\x80\x9d The other\r\nwould reply, \xe2\x80\x9cWell, one can\xe2\x80\x99t be too careful, you know.\xe2\x80\x9d The effect on\r\na house-master of humane conscience and good intent may be imagined.\r\nAgain, a man who has sincerely devoted himself to gaining the esteem of\r\nhis charges does not like to hear himself described, even at a distance,\r\nas \xe2\x80\x9cPopularity Prout\xe2\x80\x9d by a dark and scowling Celt with a fluent tongue.\r\nA rumor that stories--unusual stories--are told in the form-rooms,\r\nbetween the lights, by a boy who does not command his confidence,\r\nagitates such a man; and even elaborate and tender politeness--for the\r\ncourtesy wise-grown men offer to a bewildered child was the courtesy\r\nthat Stalky wrapped round Prout--restores not his peace of mind.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe tone of the house seems changed--changed for the worse,\xe2\x80\x9d said Prout\r\nto Harrison and Craye. \xe2\x80\x9cHave you noticed it? I don\xe2\x80\x99t for an instant\r\nimpute--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe never imputed anything; but, on the other hand, he never did anything\r\nelse, and, with the best intentions in the world, he had reduced the\r\nhouse-prefects to a state as nearly bordering on nervous irritation\r\nas healthy boys can know. Worst of all, they began at times to wonder\r\nwhether Stalky & Co. had not some truth in their often-repeated\r\nassertions that Prout was a gloomy ass.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs you know, I am not the kind of man who puts himself out for every\r\nlittle thing he hears. _I_ believe in letting the house work out their\r\nown salvation--with a light guiding hand on the reins, of course. But\r\nthere is a perceptible lack of reverence---a lower tone in matters that\r\ntouch the honor of the house, a sort of hardness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n   Oh, Prout he is a nobleman, a nobleman, a nobleman!\r\n       Our Heffy is a nobleman--\r\n       He does an awful lot,\r\n   Because his popularity\r\n   Oh, pop-u-pop-u-larity--\r\n   His giddy popularity\r\n       Would suffer did he not!\r\n\r\nThe study door stood ajar; and the song, borne by twenty clear voices,\r\ncame faint from a form-room. The victory was to Prout, but they\r\ngrudged it not. If he had broken the rules of the game by calling in the\r\nHead, they had had a good run for their money.\r\n\r\nThe Reverend John sought the earliest opportunity of talking things\r\nover. Members of a bachelor Common-room, of a school where masters\xe2\x80\x99\r\nstudies are designedly dotted among studies and form-rooms, can, if they\r\nchoose, see a great deal of their charges. Number Five had spent some\r\ncautious years in testing the Reverend John. He was emphatically a\r\ngentleman. He knocked at a study door before entering; he comported\r\nhimself as a visitor and not a strayed lictor; he never prosed, and he\r\nnever carried over into official life the confidences of idle hours.\r\nProut was ever an unmitigated nuisance; King came solely as an avenger\r\nof blood; even little Hartopp, talking natural history, seldom forgot\r\nhis office; but the Reverend John was a guest desired and beloved by\r\nNumber Five.\r\n\r\nBehold him, then, in their only arm-chair, a bent briar between his\r\nteeth, chin down in three folds on his clerical collar, and blowing like\r\nan amiable whale, while Number Five discoursed of life as it appeared to\r\nthem, and specially of that last interview with the Head--in the matter\r\nof usury.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne licking once a week would do you an immense amount of good,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n he said, twinkling and shaking all over; \xe2\x80\x9cand, as you say, you were\r\nentirely in the right.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRa-ather, Padre! That\xe2\x80\x99s what they say. Only kick their\r\nsouls out of \xe2\x80\x99em, and they go and blub in the box-rooms. Shove their\r\nheads into the ulsters an\xe2\x80\x99 blub. I\xe2\x80\x99m sorry you made _pax_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not!\xe2\x80\x9d said Campbell, who was a humorist in a way. \xe2\x80\x9cLook out, you\xe2\x80\x99re\r\nslaying Sefton!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn his excitement Beetle had used the stump unreflectingly, and Sefton\r\nwas now shouting for mercy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn\xe2\x80\x99 you!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried, wheeling where he sat. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve never been bullied,\r\neither. I\xe2\x80\x99ll give up! Let me\r\noff! I\xe2\x80\x99m broke! I can\xe2\x80\x99t stand it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUgh! Just when we were gettin\xe2\x80\x99 our hand in!\xe2\x80\x9d grunted McTurk.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey didn\xe2\x80\x99t let Clewer off, I\xe2\x80\x99ll swear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cConfess--apologize--quick!\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky.\r\n\r\nFrom the floor Sefton made unconditional surrender, more abjectly even\r\nthan Campbell. He would never touch any one again. He would go softly\r\nall the days of his life.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ve got to take it, I suppose?\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky. \xe2\x80\x9cAll right, Sefton.\r\nYou\xe2\x80\x99re broke? Campbell and Sefton looked at each\r\nother, and, neither taking comfort from that view, struck up \xe2\x80\x9cKitty of\r\nColeraine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVile bad,\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky, as the miserable wailing ended. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you hadn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nsurrendered it would have been our painful duty to buzz books at you for\r\nsingin\xe2\x80\x99 out o\xe2\x80\x99 tune. Sefton staggered\r\nto the table, buried his head in his arms, and shook with sobs. There\r\nwas no shadow of fight in either--only amazement, distress, and shame.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCa--can\xe2\x80\x99t he shave clean before tea, please?\xe2\x80\x9d said Campbell. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s ten\r\nminutes to bell.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nStalky shook his head. They were all\r\ndripping with excitement and exertion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I knew anything about it, I swear I\xe2\x80\x99d give you a moral lecture,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said Stalky severely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t jaw; they\xe2\x80\x99ve surrendered,\xe2\x80\x9d said McTurk. \xe2\x80\x9cThis moral suasion\r\nbiznai takes it out of a chap.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you see how gentle we\xe2\x80\x99ve been? We might have called Clewer in to\r\nlook at you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98The bleatin\xe2\x80\x99 of the tiger excites the kid.\xe2\x80\x99\r\nBut we didn\xe2\x80\x99t. We\xe2\x80\x99ve only got to tell a few chaps in Coll. about this\r\nand you\xe2\x80\x99d be hooted all over the shop. Your life wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be worth\r\nhavin\xe2\x80\x99. Horrid bad for the fags if they saw\r\nhim. He can shave. Ain\xe2\x80\x99t you grateful, Sefton?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe head did not lift. Sefton was deeply asleep.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s rummy,\xe2\x80\x9d said McTurk, as a snore mixed with a sob. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Cheek, _I_\r\nthink; or else he\xe2\x80\x99s shammin\xe2\x80\x99.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, \xe2\x80\x99tisn\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d said Beetle. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98When \xe2\x80\x98Molly\xe2\x80\x99 Fairburn had attended to me\r\nfor an hour or so I used to go bung off to sleep on a form sometimes.\r\nPoor devil! But he called me a beastly poet, though.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, come on.\xe2\x80\x9d Stalky lowered his voice. \xe2\x80\x9cGood-by, Campbell. \xe2\x80\x99Member,\r\nif you don\xe2\x80\x99t talk, nobody will.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere should have been a war-dance, but that all three were so utterly\r\ntired that they almost went to sleep above the tea-cups in their study,\r\nand slept till prep.\r\n\r\n       *       *       *       *       *\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA most extraordinary letter. Are all parents incurably mad? What do you\r\nmake of it?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Head, handing a closely written eight pages to the\r\nReverend John.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98The only son of his mother, and she a widow.\xe2\x80\x99 That is the least\r\nreasonable sort.\xe2\x80\x9d The chaplain read with pursed lips. \xe2\x80\x9cIf half\r\nthose charges are true he should be in the sick-house; whereas he is\r\ndisgustingly well. Certainly he has shaved. I noticed that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUnder compulsion, as his mother points out. How delicious! How\r\nsalutary!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou haven\xe2\x80\x99t to answer her. It isn\xe2\x80\x99t often I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what has\r\nhappened in the school; but this is beyond me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you asked me I should say seek not to propitiate. When one is forced\r\nto take crammers\xe2\x80\x99 pups--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe was perfectly well at extra-tuition--with me--this morning,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nthe Head, absently. \xe2\x80\x9cUnusually well behaved, too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c--they either educate the school, or the school, as in this case,\r\neducates them. And nothing excuses his trying to give the College a\r\nbad name.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the line I mean to take with him,\xe2\x80\x9d the Head answered.\r\n\r\nThe Augurs winked.\r\n\r\n       *       *       *       *       *\r\n\r\nA few days later the Reverend John called on Number Five. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy haven\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nwe seen you before, Padre?\xe2\x80\x9d said they.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve been watching times and seasons and events and men--and boys,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nreplied. \xe2\x80\x9cI am pleased with my Tenth Legion. I make them my compliments.\r\nClewer was throwing ink-balls in form this morning, instead of doing his\r\nwork. He is now doing fifty lines for--unheard-of audacity.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can\xe2\x80\x99t blame us, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said Beetle. \xe2\x80\x9cYou told us to remove\r\nthe--er--pressure. That\xe2\x80\x99s the worst of a fag.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve known boys five years his senior throw ink-balls, Beetle. If I had used one half of the moral suasion you may or\r\nmay not have employed--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith the best motives in the world. Don\xe2\x80\x99t forget our pious motives,\r\nPadre,\xe2\x80\x9d said McTurk.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose I should be now languishing in Bideford jail, shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t I?\r\nWell, to quote the Head, in a little business which we have agreed to\r\nforget, that strikes me as flagrant injustice... What are you laughing\r\nat, you young sinners? Isn\xe2\x80\x99t it true? I will not stay to be shouted at.\r\nWhat I looked into this den of iniquity for was to find out if any one\r\ncared to come down for a bathe off the Ridge. But I see you won\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWon\xe2\x80\x99t we, though! Half a shake, Padre Sahib, till we get our towels,\r\nand _nous sommes avec vous_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nA LITTLE PREP.\r\n\r\n\r\nEaster term was but a month old when Stettson major, a day-boy,\r\ncontracted diphtheria, and the Head was very angry. He decreed a new and\r\nnarrower set of bounds--the infection had been traced to an out-lying\r\nfarmhouse--urged the prefects severely to lick all trespassers, and\r\npromised extra attentions from his own hand. There were no words bad\r\nenough for Stettson major, quarantined at his mother\xe2\x80\x99s house, who had\r\nlowered the school-average of health. This he said in the gymnasium\r\nafter prayers. Then he wrote some two hundred letters to as many anxious\r\nparents and guardians, and bade the school carry on. The trouble did not\r\nspread, but, one night, a dog-cart drove to the Head\xe2\x80\x99s door, and in the\r\nmorning the Head had gone, leaving all things in charge of Mr. King,\r\nsenior house-master. The Head often ran up to town, where the school\r\ndevoutly believed he bribed officials for early proofs of the Army\r\nExamination papers; but this absence was unusually prolonged.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDowny old bird!\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky to the allies one wet afternoon in the\r\nstudy. \xe2\x80\x9cHe must have gone on a bend and been locked up under a false\r\nname.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat for?\xe2\x80\x9d Beetle entered joyously into the libel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cForty shillin\xe2\x80\x99s or a month for hackin\xe2\x80\x99 the chucker-out of the Pavvy on\r\nthe shins. Bates always has a spree when he goes to town. Wish he\r\nwas back, though. I\xe2\x80\x99m about sick o\xe2\x80\x99 King\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x98whips an\xe2\x80\x99 scorpions\xe2\x80\x99 an\xe2\x80\x99\r\nlectures on public-school spirit--yah!--and scholarship!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Crass an\xe2\x80\x99 materialized brutality of the middle-classes--readin\xe2\x80\x99 solely\r\nfor marks. Not a scholar in the whole school,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d McTurk quoted, pensively\r\nboring holes in the mantel-piece with a hot poker.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s rather a sickly way of spending an afternoon. Stinks too.\r\nLet\xe2\x80\x99s come out an\xe2\x80\x99 smoke. The match-team was worked at least four days out of\r\nseven, and the school saw hope ahead.\r\n\r\nWith the last week of the term the Old Boys began to arrive, and their\r\nwelcome was nicely proportioned to their worth. Gentlemen cadets from\r\nSandhurst and Woolwich, who had only left a year ago, but who carried\r\nenormous side, were greeted with a cheerful \xe2\x80\x9cHullo! They would walk to and fro in\r\nthe corridor with the little red school-sergeant, telling news of old\r\nregiments; they would burst into form-rooms sniffing the well-remembered\r\nsmells of ink and whitewash; they would find nephews and cousins in the\r\nlower forms and present them with enormous wealth; or they would invade\r\nthe gymnasium and make Foxy show off the new stock on the bars.\r\n\r\nChiefly, though, they talked with the Head, who was father-confessor\r\nand agent-general to them all; for what they shouted in their unthinking\r\nyouth, they proved in their thoughtless manhood--to wit, that the\r\nProoshan Bates was \xe2\x80\x9ca downy bird.\xe2\x80\x9d Young blood who had stumbled into an\r\nentanglement with a pastry-cook\xe2\x80\x99s daughter at Plymouth; experience who\r\nhad come into a small legacy but mistrusted lawyers; ambition halting\r\nat cross-roads, anxious to take the one that would lead him farthest;\r\nextravagance pursued by the money-lender; arrogance in the thick of a\r\nregimental row--each carried his trouble to the Head; and Chiron showed\r\nhim, in language quite unfit for little boys, a quiet and safe way\r\nround, out, or under. That\xe2\x80\x99s all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen he\xe2\x80\x99s a brave man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWould _you_ funk it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRa-ather. Anybody would. Fancy dying of diphtheria in cold blood.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell--ah! Er! Look here!\xe2\x80\x9d The sentence ended in a grunt, for Stalky had\r\nleaped out of bed and with McTurk was sitting on the head of Beetle, who\r\nwould have sprung the mine there and then.\r\n\r\nNext day, which was the last of the term and given up to a few wholly\r\nunimportant examinations, began with wrath and war. Mr. King had\r\ndiscovered that nearly all his house--it lay, as you know, next door but\r\none to Prout\xe2\x80\x99s in the long range of buildings--had unlocked the doors\r\nbetween the dormitories and had gone in to listen to a story told by\r\nCrandall. He went to the Head, clamorous, injured, appealing; for\r\nhe never approved of allowing so-called young men of the world to\r\ncontaminate the morals of boyhood. Very good, said the Head, he would\r\nattend to it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x99m awf\xe2\x80\x99ly sorry,\xe2\x80\x9d said Crandall guiltily. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think I told\r\n\xe2\x80\x99em anything they oughtn\xe2\x80\x99t to hear. Don\xe2\x80\x99t let them get into trouble on\r\nmy account.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTck!\xe2\x80\x9d the Head answered, with the ghost of a wink. \xe2\x80\x9cIt isn\xe2\x80\x99t the boys\r\nthat make trouble; it\xe2\x80\x99s the masters. However, Prout and King don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\napprove of dormitory gatherings on this scale, and one must back up the\r\nhouse-masters. Moreover, it\xe2\x80\x99s hopeless to punish two houses only, so\r\nlate in the term. We must be fair and include everybody. Let\xe2\x80\x99s see. They\r\nhave a holiday task for the Easters, which, of course, none of them\r\nwill ever look at. We will give the whole school, except prefects and\r\nstudy-boys, regular prep. to-night; and the Common-room will have to\r\nsupply a master to take it. We must be fair to all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPrep. on the last night of the term. Whew!\xe2\x80\x9d said Crandall, thinking of\r\nhis own wild youth. \xe2\x80\x9cI fancy there will be larks.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe school, frolicking among packed trunks, whooping down the corridor,\r\nand \xe2\x80\x9cgloating\xe2\x80\x9d in form-rooms, received the news with amazement and rage.\r\nNo school in the world did prep. on the last night of the term. This\r\nthing was monstrous, tyrannical, subversive of law, religion, and\r\nmorality. They would go into the form-rooms, and they would take their\r\ndegraded holiday task with them, but--here they smiled and speculated\r\nwhat manner of man the Common-room would send up against them. The lot\r\nfell on Mason, credulous and enthusiastic, who loved youth. No other\r\nmaster was anxious to take that \xe2\x80\x9cprep.,\xe2\x80\x9d for the school lacked the\r\nsteadying influence of tradition; and men accustomed to the ordered\r\nroutine of ancient foundations found it occasionally insubordinate. The\r\nfour long form-rooms, in which all below the rank of study-boys worked,\r\nreceived him with thunders of applause. Ere he had coughed twice they\r\nfavored him with a metrical summary of the marriage laws of Great\r\nBritain, as recorded by the High Priest of the Israelites and commented\r\non by the leader of the host. The lower forms reminded him that it was\r\nthe last day, and that therefore he must \xe2\x80\x9ctake it all in play.\xe2\x80\x9d When he\r\ndashed off to rebuke them, the Lower Fourth and Upper Third began with\r\none accord to be sick, loudly and realistically. Mr. Mason tried, of all\r\nvain things under heaven, to argue with them, and a bold soul at a\r\nback desk bade him \xe2\x80\x9ctake fifty lines for not \xe2\x80\x99olding up \xe2\x80\x99is \xe2\x80\x99and before\r\nspeaking.\xe2\x80\x9d As one who prided himself upon the perfection of his English\r\nthis cut Mason to the quick, and while he was trying to discover the\r\noffender, the Upper and Lower Second, three form-rooms away, turned out\r\nthe gas and threw ink-pots. It was a pleasant and stimulating \xe2\x80\x9cprep.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n The study-boys and prefects heard the echoes of it far off, and the\r\nCommon-room at dessert smiled.\r\n\r\nStalky waited, watch in hand, till half-past eight. \xe2\x80\x9cIf it goes on much\r\nlonger the Head will come up,\xe2\x80\x9d said he. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll tell the studies first,\r\nand then the dorm-rooms. Look sharp!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe allowed no time for Beetle to be dramatic or McTurk to drawl. They\r\npoured into study after study, told their tale, and went again so soon\r\nas they saw they were understood, waiting for no comment; while the\r\nnoise of that unholy \xe2\x80\x9cprep.\xe2\x80\x9d grew and deepened. By the door of Flint\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nstudy they met Mason flying towards the corridor.--\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s gone to fetch\r\nthe Head. Hurry up! Come on!\xe2\x80\x9d They broke into Number Twelve form-room\r\nabreast and panting.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Head! The Head! The Head!\xe2\x80\x9d That call stilled the tumult for a\r\nminute, and Stalky, leaping to a desk, shouted, \xe2\x80\x9cHe went and sucked the\r\ndiphtheria stuff out of Stettson major\xe2\x80\x99s throat when we thought he was\r\nin town. Stop rotting, you asses! Stettson major would have croaked if\r\nthe Head hadn\xe2\x80\x99t done it. The Head might have died himself. Crandall\r\nsays it\xe2\x80\x99s the bravest thing any livin\xe2\x80\x99 man can do, and I\xe2\x80\x9d--his voice\r\ncracked--\xe2\x80\x9cthe Head don\xe2\x80\x99t know we know!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMcTurk and Beetle, jumping from desk to desk, drove the news home among\r\nthe junior forms. There was a pause, and then, Mason behind him, the\r\nHead entered. It was in the established order of things that no boy\r\nshould speak or move under his eye. He expected the hush of awe. He was\r\nreceived with cheers--steady, ceaseless cheering. Being a wise man, he\r\nwent away, and the forms were silent and a little frightened.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s all right,\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky. \xe2\x80\x9cHe can\xe2\x80\x99t do much. \xe2\x80\x99Tisn\xe2\x80\x99t as if you\xe2\x80\x99d\r\npulled the desks up like we did when old Carleton took prep. once. Keep\r\nit up! Hear \xe2\x80\x99em cheering in the studies!\xe2\x80\x9d He rocketed out with a yell,\r\nto find Flint and the prefects lifting the roof off the corridor.\r\n\r\nWhen the Head of a limited liability company, paying four per cent., is\r\ncheered on his saintly way to prayers, not only by four form-rooms of\r\nboys waiting punishment, but by his trusted prefects, he can either\r\nask for an explanation or go his road with dignity, while the senior\r\nhouse-master glares like an excited cat and points out to a white and\r\ntrembling mathematical master that certain methods--not his, thank\r\nGod---usually produce certain results. Out of delicacy the Old Boys\r\ndid not attend that call-over; and it was to the school drawn up in the\r\ngymnasium that the Head spoke icily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is not often that I do not understand you; but I confess I do not\r\nto-night. Some of you, after your idiotic performances at prep., seem to\r\nthink me a fit person to cheer. I am going to show you that I am not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCrash--crash--crash--came the triple cheer that disproved it, and the\r\nHead glowered under the gas. \xe2\x80\x9cThat is enough. You will gain nothing. The\r\nlittle boys (the Lower School did not like that form of address) will do\r\nme three hundred lines apiece in the holidays. I shall take no further\r\nnotice of them. The Upper School will do me one thousand lines apiece in\r\nthe holidays, to be shown up the evening of the day they come back. And\r\nfurther--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGummy, what a glutton!\xe2\x80\x9d Stalky whispered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor your behavior towards Mr. Mason I intend to lick the whole of the\r\nUpper School to-morrow when I give you your journey-money. This will\r\ninclude the three study-boys I found dancing on the form-room desks when\r\nI came up. Prefects will stay after call-over.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe school filed out in silence, but gathered in groups by the gymnasium\r\ndoor waiting what might befall.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd now, Flint,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Head, \xe2\x80\x9cwill you be good enough to give me\r\nsome explanation of your conduct?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said Flint desperately, \xe2\x80\x9cif you save a chap\xe2\x80\x99s life at the\r\nrisk of your own when he\xe2\x80\x99s dyin\xe2\x80\x99 of diphtheria, and the Coll. finds it\r\nout, wha-what can you expect, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUm, I see. I\xe2\x80\x99ll forego the lines this once, remember;\r\nbut the lickings hold good.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen this news was made public, the school, lost in wonder and\r\nadmiration, gasped at the Head as he went to his house. Here was a man\r\nto be reverenced. On the rare occasions when he caned he did it\r\nvery scientifically, and the execution of a hundred boys would be\r\nepic--immense.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s all right, Head Sahib. _We_ know,\xe2\x80\x9d said Crandall, as the Head\r\nslipped off his gown with a grunt in his smoking-room. \xe2\x80\x9cI found out just\r\nnow from our substitute. Besides, the\r\nmen at the golf-club heard them singing \xe2\x80\x98Aaron and Moses.\xe2\x80\x99 I shall have\r\ncomplaints about that from the parents of day-boys. Decency must be\r\npreserved.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re coming to help,\xe2\x80\x9d said all the guests.\r\n\r\n       *       *       *       *       *\r\n\r\nThe Upper School were caned one after the other, their overcoats over\r\ntheir arms, the brakes waiting in the road below to take them to the\r\nstation, their journey-money on the table. The Head began with Stalky,\r\nMcTurk, and Beetle. He dealt faithfully by them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd here\xe2\x80\x99s your journey-money. Then they seriously devoted\r\nthemselves to cheering till the brakes were hustled off the premises in\r\ndumb-show.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDidn\xe2\x80\x99t I say I\xe2\x80\x99d get even with him?\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky on the box-seat, as\r\nthey swung into the narrow Northam street. \xe2\x80\x9cNow all together--takin\xe2\x80\x99\r\ntime from your Uncle Stalky:\r\n\r\n   It\xe2\x80\x99s a way we have in the Army,\r\n   It\xe2\x80\x99s a way we have in the Navy,\r\n   It\xe2\x80\x99s a way we have at the Public Schools,\r\n   Which nobody can deny!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nTHE FLAG OF THEIR COUNTRY.\r\n\r\n\r\nIt was winter and bitter cold of mornings. Consequently Stalky and\r\nBeetle--McTurk being of the offensive type that makes ornate toilet\r\nunder all circumstances--drowsed till the last moment before turning out\r\nto call-over in the gas-lit gymnasium. It followed that they were often\r\nlate; and since every unpunctuality earned them a black mark, and since\r\nthree black marks a week meant defaulters\xe2\x80\x99 drill, equally it followed\r\nthat they spent hours under the Sergeant\xe2\x80\x99s hand. Foxy drilled the\r\ndefaulters with all the pomp of his old parade-ground. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t think it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nany pleasure to me\xe2\x80\x9d (his introduction never varied). \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d much sooner\r\nbe smoking a quiet pipe in my own quarters--but I see we \xe2\x80\x99ave the Old\r\nBrigade on our \xe2\x80\x99ands this afternoon. If I only \xe2\x80\x99ad you regular, Muster\r\nCorkran,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, dressing the line.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve had me for nearly six weeks, you old glutton. Number off from\r\nthe right!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot _quite_ so previous, please. I\xe2\x80\x99m taking this drill. Left,\r\nhalf--turn! Slow--march.\xe2\x80\x9d Twenty-five sluggards, all old offenders,\r\nfiled into the gymnasium. \xe2\x80\x9cQuietly provide yourselves with the requisite\r\ndumb-bells; returnin\xe2\x80\x99 quietly to your place. Number off from the right,\r\nin a low voice. Odd numbers one pace to the front. Even numbers stand\r\nfast. Now, leanin\xe2\x80\x99 forward from the \xe2\x80\x99ips, takin\xe2\x80\x99 your time from me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe dumb-bells rose and fell, clashed and were returned as one. The boys\r\nwere experts at the weary game.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVe-ry good. I shall be sorry when any of you resume your \xe2\x80\x99abits of\r\npunctuality. Quietly return dumb-bells. We will now try some simple\r\ndrill.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUgh! I know that simple drill.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt would he \xe2\x80\x99ighly to your discredit if you did not, Muster Corkran.\r\n_At_ the same time, it is not so easy as it looks.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBet you a bob, I can drill as well as you, Foxy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll see later. As Foxy said, they had been at\r\nit a long time.\r\n\r\nThe gymnasium door opened, revealing McTurk in charge of an old\r\ngentleman.\r\n\r\nThe Sergeant, leading a wheel, did not see. \xe2\x80\x9cNot so bad,\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot \xe2\x80\x99arf so bad. Your first\r\ndrill--eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d He drew an unhappy breath. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Tention. Dress!\xe2\x80\x9d The echo of\r\nhis own voice restored his confidence.\r\n\r\nThe wheel was faced about, flung back, broken into fours, and restored\r\nto line without a falter. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know when I\xe2\x80\x99ve been so pleased. Boys\r\nanimated by a spirit like yours should set an example to the whole\r\nschool.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey do,\xe2\x80\x9d said McTurk.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBless my soul! Thrice had the guard been maltreated and thrice had the\r\ncorps dealt out martial law to the offender. The school raged. What was\r\nthe use, they asked, of a cadet-corps which none might see? Mr. King\r\ncongratulated them on their invisible defenders, and they could not\r\nparry his thrusts. Foxy was growing sullen and restive. If these were issued,\r\nthey would be forced to wear them.\r\n\r\nBut, as so often happens in this life, the matter was suddenly settled\r\nfrom without.\r\n\r\nThe Head had duly informed the Council that their recommendation had\r\nbeen acted upon, and that, so far as he could learn, the boys were\r\ndrilling. He said nothing of the terms on which they drilled. Naturally,\r\nGeneral Collinson was delighted and told his friends. One of his friends\r\nrejoiced in a friend, a Member of Parliament--a zealous, an intelligent,\r\nand, above all, a patriotic person, anxious to do the most good in the\r\nshortest possible time. But we cannot answer, alas! for the friends of\r\nour friends. If Collinson\xe2\x80\x99s friend had introduced him to the General,\r\nthe latter would have taken his measure and saved much. P\xe2\x80\x99raps he\xe2\x80\x99ll hiccough.\r\nWhoever gets into the Gym first, bags seats for the other two.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n       *       *       *       *       *\r\n\r\nThere was no nervousness, but a brisk and cheery affability about Mr.\r\nRaymond Martin, M.P., as he drove up, watched by many eyes, to the\r\nHead\xe2\x80\x99s house.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLooks a bit of a bargee,\xe2\x80\x9d was McTurk\xe2\x80\x99s comment. \xe2\x80\x9cShouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be surprised\r\nif he was a Radical. When Mr. Martin took the applause to himself, they\r\nnaturally applauded more than ever. It was some time before he could\r\nbegin. He had no knowledge of the school--its tradition or heritage. He\r\ndid not know that the last census showed that eighty per cent. of the\r\nboys had been born abroad--in camp, cantonment, or upon the high seas;\r\nor that seventy-five per cent. were sons of officers in one or other of\r\nthe services--Willoughbys, Paulets, De Castros, Maynes, Randalls, after\r\ntheir kind--looking to follow their fathers\xe2\x80\x99 profession. The Head might\r\nhave told him this, and much more; but, after an hour-long dinner in his\r\ncompany, the Head decided to say nothing whatever. Mr. Raymond Martin\r\nseemed to know so much already.\r\n\r\nHe plunged into his speech with a long-drawn, rasping \xe2\x80\x9cWell, boys,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n that, though they were not conscious of it, set every young nerve ajar.\r\nHe supposed they knew--hey?--what he had come down for? It was not often\r\nthat he had an opportunity to talk to boys. He supposed that boys were\r\nvery much the same kind of persons--some people thought them rather\r\nfunny persons--as they had been in his youth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis man,\xe2\x80\x9d said McTurk, with conviction, \xe2\x80\x9cis _the_ Gadarene Swine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut they must remember that they would not always be boys. They would\r\ngrow up into men, because the boys of to-day made the men of to-morrow,\r\nand upon the men of to-morrow the fair fame of their glorious native\r\nland depended.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf this goes on, my beloved \xe2\x80\x99earers, it will be my painful duty to rot\r\nthis bargee.\xe2\x80\x9d Stalky drew a long breath through his nose.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan\xe2\x80\x99t do that,\xe2\x80\x9d said McTurk. \xe2\x80\x9cHe ain\xe2\x80\x99t chargin\xe2\x80\x99 anything for his\r\nRomeo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd so they ought to think of the duties and responsibilities of the\r\nlife that was opening before them. Life was not all--he enumerated a few\r\ngames, and, that nothing might be lacking to the sweep and impact of his\r\nfall, added \xe2\x80\x9cmarbles.\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cYes, life was not,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9call marbles.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was one tense gasp--among the juniors almost a shriek--of\r\nquivering horror, he was a heathen--an outcast---beyond the extremest\r\npale of toleration--self-damned before all men. Stalky bowed his head in\r\nhis hands. McTurk, with a bright and cheerful eye, drank in every word,\r\nand Beetle nodded solemn approval.\r\n\r\nSome of them, doubtless, expected in a few years to have the honor of a\r\ncommission from the Queen, and to wear a sword. Now, he himself had had\r\nsome experience of these duties, as a Major in a volunteer regiment,\r\nand he was glad to learn that they had established a volunteer corps in\r\ntheir midst. The establishment of such an establishment conduced to a\r\nproper and healthy spirit, which, if fostered, would be of great benefit\r\nto the land they loved and were so proud to belong to. Some of those now\r\npresent expected, he had no doubt--some of them anxiously looked\r\nforward to leading their men against the bullets of England\xe2\x80\x99s foes;\r\nto confronting the stricken field in all the pride of their youthful\r\nmanhood.\r\n\r\nNow the reserve of a boy is tenfold deeper than the reserve of a maid,\r\nshe being made for one end only by blind Nature, but man for several.\r\nWith a large and healthy hand, he tore down these veils, and trampled\r\nthem under the well-intentioned feet of eloquence. In a raucous voice,\r\nhe cried aloud little matters, like the hope of Honor and the dream of\r\nGlory, that boys do not discuss even with their most intimate equals,\r\ncheerfully assuming that, till he spoke, they had never considered these\r\npossibilities. He pointed them to shining goals, with fingers which\r\nsmudged out all radiance on all horizons. He profaned the most secret\r\nplaces of their souls with outcries and gesticulations, he bade them\r\nconsider the deeds of their ancestors in such a fashion that they were\r\nflushed to their tingling ears. Some of them--the rending voice cut a\r\nfrozen stillness--might have had relatives who perished in defence of\r\ntheir country. They thought, not a few of them, of an old sword in a\r\npassage, or above a breakfast-room table, seen and fingered by stealth\r\nsince they could walk. He adjured them to emulate those illustrious\r\nexamples; and they looked all ways in their extreme discomfort.\r\n\r\nTheir years forbade them even to shape their thoughts clearly to\r\nthemselves. They felt savagely that they were being outraged by a fat\r\nman who considered marbles a game.\r\n\r\nAnd so he worked towards his peroration--which, by the way, he used\r\nlater with overwhelming success at a meeting of electors--while they\r\nsat, flushed and uneasy, in sour disgust. After many, many words, he\r\nreached for the cloth-wrapped stick and thrust one hand in his bosom.\r\nThis--this was the concrete symbol of their land--worthy of all honor\r\nand reverence! Let no boy look on this flag who did not purpose to\r\nworthily add to its imperishable lustre. He shook it before them--a\r\nlarge calico Union Jack, staring in all three colors, and waited for the\r\nthunder of applause that should crown his effort.\r\n\r\nThey looked in silence. They had certainly seen the thing before--down\r\nat the coastguard station, or through a telescope, half-mast high when a\r\nbrig went ashore on Braunton Sands; above the roof of the Golf-club, and\r\nin Keyte\xe2\x80\x99s window, where a certain kind of striped sweetmeat bore it in\r\npaper on each box. But the College never displayed it; it was no part\r\nof the scheme of their lives; the Head had never alluded to it; their\r\nfathers had not declared it unto them. It was a matter shut up, sacred\r\nand apart. What, in the name of everything caddish, was he driving at,\r\nwho waved that horror before their eyes? Happy thought! Perhaps he was\r\ndrunk.\r\n\r\nThe Head saved the situation by rising swiftly to propose a vote of\r\nthanks, and at his first motion, the school clapped furiously, from a\r\nsense of relief.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I am sure,\xe2\x80\x9d he concluded, the gaslight full on his face, \xe2\x80\x9cthat you\r\nwill all join me in a very hearty vote of thanks to Mr. Raymond Martin\r\nfor the most enjoyable address he has given us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTo this day we shall never know the rights of the case. The Head\r\nvows that he did no such thing; or that, if he did, it must have been\r\nsomething in his eye; but those who were present are persuaded that\r\nhe winked, once, openly and solemnly, after the word \xe2\x80\x9cenjoyable.\xe2\x80\x9d Mr.\r\nRaymond Martin got his applause full tale. As he said, \xe2\x80\x9cWithout vanity,\r\nI think my few words went to their hearts. I never knew boys could cheer\r\nlike that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe left as the prayer-bell rang, and the boys lined up against the wall.\r\nThe flag lay still unrolled on the desk, Foxy regarding it with pride,\r\nfor he had been touched to the quick by Mr. Martin\xe2\x80\x99s eloquence. The\r\nHead and the Common-room, standing back on the dais, could not see the\r\nglaring offence, but a prefect left the line, rolled it up swiftly, and\r\nas swiftly tossed it into a glove and foil locker.\r\n\r\nThen, as though he had touched a spring, broke out the low murmur of\r\ncontent, changing to quick-volleyed hand-clapping.\r\n\r\nThey discussed the speech in the dormitories. There was not one\r\ndissentient voice. Mr. Raymond Martin, beyond question, was born in a\r\ngutter, and bred in a board-school, where they played marbles. He was\r\nfurther (I give the barest handful from great store) a Flopshus Cad,\r\nan Outrageous Stinker, a Jelly-bellied Flag-flapper (this was Stalky\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncontribution), and several other things which it is not seemly to put\r\ndown.\r\n\r\nThe volunteer cadet-corps fell in next Monday, depressedly, with a face\r\nof shame. Even then, judicious silence might have turned the corner.\r\n\r\nSaid Foxy: \xe2\x80\x9cAfter a fine speech like what you \xe2\x80\x99eard night before last,\r\nyou ought to take \xe2\x80\x99old of your drill with _re_-newed activity. I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nsee how you can avoid comin\xe2\x80\x99 out an\xe2\x80\x99 marchin\xe2\x80\x99 in the open now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan\xe2\x80\x99t we get out of it, then, Foxy?\xe2\x80\x9d Stalky\xe2\x80\x99s fine old silky tone\r\nshould have warned him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, not with his giving the flag so generously. He told me before he\r\nleft this morning that there was no objection to the corps usin\xe2\x80\x99 it as\r\ntheir own. It\xe2\x80\x99s a handsome flag.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nStalky returned his rifle to the rack in dead silence, and fell out. His\r\nexample was followed by Hogan and Ansell. Perowne hesitated. \xe2\x80\x9cLook here,\r\noughtn\xe2\x80\x99t we--?\xe2\x80\x9d he began.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll get it out of the locker in a minute,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Sergeant, his back\r\nturned. \xe2\x80\x9cThen we can--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome on!\xe2\x80\x9d shouted Stalky. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat the devil are you waiting for? Dismiss!\r\nBreak off.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy--what the--where the--?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe rattle of Sniders, slammed into the rack, drowned his voice, as boy\r\nafter boy fell out.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI--I don\xe2\x80\x99t know that I shan\xe2\x80\x99t have to report this to the Head,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nstammered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReport, then, and be damned to you,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Stalky, white to the lips,\r\nand ran out.\r\n\r\n       *       *       *       *       *\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRummy thing!\xe2\x80\x9d said Beetle to McTurk. \xe2\x80\x9cI was in the study, doin\xe2\x80\x99 a\r\nsimply lovely poem about the Jelly-Bellied Flag-Flapper, an\xe2\x80\x99 Stalky\r\ncame in, an\xe2\x80\x99 I said \xe2\x80\x98Hullo!\xe2\x80\x99 an\xe2\x80\x99 he cursed me like a bargee, and then\r\nhe began to blub like anything. Shoved his head on the table and howled.\r\nHadn\xe2\x80\x99t we better do something?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMcTurk was troubled. \xe2\x80\x9cP\xe2\x80\x99raps he\xe2\x80\x99s smashed himself up somehow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey found him, with very bright eyes, whistling between his teeth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid I take you in, Beetle? I thought I would. Wasn\xe2\x80\x99t it a good draw?\r\nDidn\xe2\x80\x99t you think I was blubbin\xe2\x80\x99? Didn\xe2\x80\x99t I do it well? Oh, you fat old\r\nass!\xe2\x80\x9d And he began to pull Beetle\xe2\x80\x99s ears and checks, in the fashion that\r\nwas called \xe2\x80\x9cmilking.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI knew you were blubbin\xe2\x80\x99,\xe2\x80\x9d Beetle replied, composedly. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy aren\xe2\x80\x99t you\r\nat drill?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDrill! What drill?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t try to be a clever fool. Drill in the Gym.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Cause there isn\xe2\x80\x99t any. The volunteer cadet-corps is broke\r\nup--disbanded--dead--putrid--corrupt---stinkin\xe2\x80\x99. An\xe2\x80\x99 if you look at me\r\nlike that, Beetle, I\xe2\x80\x99ll slay you too... Oh, yes, an\xe2\x80\x99 I\xe2\x80\x99m goin\xe2\x80\x99 to be\r\nreported to the Head for swearin\xe2\x80\x99.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nTHE LAST TERM.\r\n\r\n\r\nIt was within a few days of the holidays, the term-end examinations,\r\nand, more important still, the issue of the College paper which Beetle\r\nedited. He had been cajoled into that office by the blandishments of\r\nStalky and McTurk and the extreme rigor of study law. Once installed, he\r\ndiscovered, as others have done before him, that his duty was to do\r\nthe work while his friends criticized. Stalky christened it the\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSwillingford Patriot,\xe2\x80\x9d in pious memory of Sponge--and McTurk compared\r\nthe output unfavorably with Ruskin and De Quincey. Only the Head took\r\nan interest in the publication, and his methods were peculiar. He gave\r\nBeetle the run of his brown-bound, tobacco-scented library; prohibiting\r\nnothing, recommending nothing. There Beetle found a fat arm-chair, a\r\nsilver inkstand, and unlimited pens and paper. There were scores and\r\nscores of ancient dramatists; there were Hakluyt, his voyages; French\r\ntranslations of Muscovite authors called Pushkin and Lermontoff; little\r\ntales of a heady and bewildering nature, interspersed with unusual\r\nsongs--Peacock was that writer\xe2\x80\x99s name; there was Borrow\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cLavengro\xe2\x80\x9d;\r\nan odd theme, purporting to be a translation of something, called a\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRuba\xe2\x80\x99iyat,\xe2\x80\x9d which the Head said was a poem not yet come to its own;\r\nthere were hundreds of volumes of verse---Crashaw; Dryden; Alexander\r\nSmith; L. E. L.; Lydia Sigourney; Fletcher and a purple island; Donne;\r\nMarlowe\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cFaust\xe2\x80\x9d; and--this made McTurk (to whom Beetle conveyed it)\r\nsheer drunk for three days--Ossian; \xe2\x80\x9cThe Earthly Paradise\xe2\x80\x9d; \xe2\x80\x9cAtalanta in\r\nCalydon\xe2\x80\x9d; and Rossetti--to name only a few. Then the Head, drifting in\r\nunder pretense of playing censor to the paper, would read here a\r\nverse and here another of these poets, opening up avenues. And, slow\r\nbreathing, with half-shut eyes above his cigar, would he speak of great\r\nmen living, and journals, long dead, founded in their riotous youth;\r\nof years when all the planets were little new-lit stars trying to find\r\ntheir places in the uncaring void, and he, the Head, knew them as young\r\nmen know one another. So the regular work went to the dogs, Beetle being\r\nfull of other matters and meters, hoarded in secret and only told to\r\nMcTurk of an afternoon, on the sands, walking high and disposedly round\r\nthe wreck of the Armada galleon, shouting and declaiming against the\r\nlong-ridged seas.\r\n\r\nThanks in large part to their house-master\xe2\x80\x99s experienced distrust, the\r\nthree for three consecutive terms had been passed over for promotion to\r\nthe rank of prefect--an office that went by merit, and carried with it\r\nthe honor of the ground-ash, and liberty, under restrictions, to use it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_But_,\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky, \xe2\x80\x9ccome to think of it, we\xe2\x80\x99ve done more giddy\r\njesting with the Sixth since we\xe2\x80\x99ve been passed over than any one else in\r\nthe last seven years.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe touched his neck proudly. It was encircled by the stiffest of\r\nstick-up collars, which custom decreed could be worn only by the Sixth.\r\nAnd the Sixth saw those collars and said no word. \xe2\x80\x9cPussy,\xe2\x80\x9d Abanazar, or\r\nDick Four of a year ago would have seen them discarded in five minutes\r\nor... But the Sixth of that term was made up mostly of young but\r\nbrilliantly clever boys, pets of the house-masters, too anxious for\r\ntheir dignity to care to come to open odds with the resourceful three.\r\nSo they crammed their caps at the extreme back of their heads,\r\ninstead of a trifle over one eye as the Fifth should, and rejoiced\r\nin patent-leather boots on week-days, and marvellous made-up ties on\r\nSundays--no man rebuking. McTurk was going up for Cooper\xe2\x80\x99s Hill, and\r\nStalky for Sandhurst, in the spring; and the Head had told them both\r\nthat, unless they absolutely collapsed during the holidays, they were\r\nsafe. As a trainer of colts, the Head seldom erred in an estimate of\r\nform.\r\n\r\nHe had taken Beetle aside that day and given him much good advice, not\r\none word of which did Beetle remember when he dashed up to the study,\r\nwhite with excitement, and poured out the wondrous tale. It demanded a\r\ngreat belief.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou begin on a hundred a year?\xe2\x80\x9d said McTurk unsympathetically. \xe2\x80\x9cRot!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_And_ my passage out! It\xe2\x80\x99s all settled. The Head says he\xe2\x80\x99s been\r\nbreaking me in for this for ever so long, and I never knew--I never\r\nknew. One don\xe2\x80\x99t begin with writing straight off, y\xe2\x80\x99know. Begin by\r\nfilling in telegrams and cutting things out o\xe2\x80\x99 papers with scissors.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Scissors! What an ungodly mess you\xe2\x80\x99ll make of it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, anyhow, this will be your last term, too. Seven years, my dearly\r\nbeloved \xe2\x80\x99earers--though not prefects.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot half bad years, either,\xe2\x80\x9d said McTurk. \xe2\x80\x9cI shall be sorry to leave\r\nthe old Coll.; shan\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey looked out over the sea creaming along the Pebbleridge in the clear\r\nwinter light. \xe2\x80\x9cWonder where we shall all be this time next year?\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nStalky absently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis time five years,\xe2\x80\x9d said McTurk.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh,\xe2\x80\x9d said Beetle, \xe2\x80\x9cmy leavin\xe2\x80\x99s between ourselves. The Head hasn\xe2\x80\x99t told\r\nany one. I know he hasn\xe2\x80\x99t, because Prout grunted at me to-day that if\r\nI were more reasonable--yah!--I might be a prefect next term. I s\xe2\x80\x99ppose\r\nhe\xe2\x80\x99s hard up for his prefects.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s finish up with a row with the Sixth,\xe2\x80\x9d suggested McTurk.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDirty little schoolboys!\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky, who already saw himself a\r\nSandhurst cadet. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the use?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMoral effect,\xe2\x80\x9d quoth McTurk. \xe2\x80\x9cLeave an imperishable tradition, and all\r\nthe rest of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBetter go into Bideford an\xe2\x80\x99 pay up our debts,\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve got\r\nthree quid out of my father--_ad hoc_. Don\xe2\x80\x99t owe more than thirty bob,\r\neither. Cut along, Beetle, and ask the Head for leave. Say you want to\r\ncorrect the \xe2\x80\x98Swillingford Patriot.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I do,\xe2\x80\x9d said Beetle. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99ll be my last issue, and I\xe2\x80\x99d like it to\r\nlook decent. I\xe2\x80\x99ll catch him before he goes to his lunch.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTen minutes later they wheeled out in line, by grace released from\r\nfive o\xe2\x80\x99clock call-over, and all the afternoon lay before them. So also\r\nunluckily did King, who never passed without witticisms. But brigades of\r\nKings could not have ruffled Beetle that day.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAha! Enjoying the study of light literature, my friends,\xe2\x80\x9d said he,\r\nrubbing his hands. \xe2\x80\x9cCommon mathematics are not for such soaring minds as\r\nyours, are they?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n(\xe2\x80\x9cOne hundred a year,\xe2\x80\x9d thought Beetle, smiling into vacancy.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOur open incompetence takes refuge in the flowery paths of inaccurate\r\nfiction. But a day of reckoning approaches, Beetle mine. I myself\r\nhave prepared a few trifling foolish questions in Latin prose which\r\ncan hardly be evaded even by your practised acts of deception. Ye-es,\r\nLatin prose. I think, if I may say so--but we shall see when the papers\r\nare set--\xe2\x80\x98Ulpian serves _your_ need.\xe2\x80\x99 Aha! \xe2\x80\x98_Elucescebat_, quoth our\r\nfriend.\xe2\x80\x99 We shall see! We shall see!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nStill no sign from Beetle. He was on a steamer, his passage paid into\r\nthe wide and wonderful world--a thousand leagues beyond Lundy Island.\r\n\r\nKing dropped him with a snarl.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe doesn\xe2\x80\x99t know. He\xe2\x80\x99ll go on correctin\xe2\x80\x99 exercises an\xe2\x80\x99 jawin\xe2\x80\x99 an\xe2\x80\x99\r\nshowin\xe2\x80\x99 off before the little boys next term--and next.\xe2\x80\x9d Beetle hurried\r\nafter his companions up the steep path of the furze-clad hill behind the\r\nCollege.\r\n\r\nThey were throwing pebbles on the top of the gasometer, and the grimy\r\ngas-man in charge bade them desist. They watched him oil a turncock sunk\r\nin the ground between two furze-bushes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCokey, what\xe2\x80\x99s that for?\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo turn the gas on to the kitchens,\xe2\x80\x9d said Cokey. \xe2\x80\x9cIf so be I\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t turn her on, yeou young gen\xe2\x80\x99lemen \xe2\x80\x99ud be larnin\xe2\x80\x99 your book by\r\ncandlelight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUm!\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky, and was silent for at least a minute.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHullo! Where are you chaps going?\xe2\x80\x9d A bend of the lane brought them\r\nface to face with Tulke, senior prefect of King\xe2\x80\x99s house--a smallish,\r\nwhite-haired boy, of the type that must be promoted on account of\r\nits intellect, and ever afterwards appeals to the Head to support its\r\nauthority when zeal has outrun discretion.\r\n\r\nThe three took no sort of notice. They were on lawful pass. Tulke\r\nrepeated his question hotly, for he had suffered many slights from\r\nNumber Five study, and fancied that he had at last caught them tripping.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat the devil is that to you?\xe2\x80\x9d Stalky replied with his sweetest smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook here, I\xe2\x80\x99m not goin\xe2\x80\x99--I\xe2\x80\x99m not goin\xe2\x80\x99 to be sworn at by the Fifth!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n sputtered Tulke.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen cut along and call a prefects\xe2\x80\x99 meeting,\xe2\x80\x9d said McTurk, knowing\r\nTulke\xe2\x80\x99s weakness.\r\n\r\nThe prefect became inarticulate with rage.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMustn\xe2\x80\x99t yell at the Fifth that way,\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s vile bad form.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCough it up, ducky!\xe2\x80\x9d McTurk said calmly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI--I want to know what you chaps are doing out of bounds?\xe2\x80\x9d This with an\r\nimportant flourish of his ground-ash.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh,\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky. \xe2\x80\x9cNow we\xe2\x80\x99re gettin\xe2\x80\x99 at it. Why didn\xe2\x80\x99t you ask that\r\nbefore?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I ask it now. What are you doing?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re admiring you, Tulke,\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky. \xe2\x80\x9cWe think you\xe2\x80\x99re no end of a\r\nfine chap, don\xe2\x80\x99t we?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe do! We do!\xe2\x80\x9d A dog-cart with some girls in it swept round the corner,\r\nand Stalky promptly kneeled before Tulke in the attitude of prayer; so\r\nTulke turned a color.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve reason to believe--\xe2\x80\x9d he began.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOyez! Oyez! Oyez!\xe2\x80\x9d shouted Beetle, after the manner of Bideford\xe2\x80\x99s town\r\ncrier, \xe2\x80\x9cTulke has reason to believe! Three cheers for Tulke!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey were given. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s all our giddy admiration,\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\nknow how we love you, Tulke. We love you so much we think you ought to\r\ngo home and die. You\xe2\x80\x99re too good to live, Tulke.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said McTurk. \xe2\x80\x9c_Do_ oblige us by dyin\xe2\x80\x99. Think how lovely you\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nlook stuffed!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTulke swept up the road with an unpleasant glare in his eye.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat means a prefects\xe2\x80\x99 meeting--sure pop,\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky. \xe2\x80\x9cHonor of the\r\nSixth involved, and all the rest of it. Tulke\xe2\x80\x99ll write notes all this\r\nafternoon, and Carson will call us up after tea. They daren\xe2\x80\x99t overlook\r\nthat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBet you a bob he follows us!\xe2\x80\x9d said McTurk. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s King\xe2\x80\x99s pet, and it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nscalps to both of \xe2\x80\x99em if we\xe2\x80\x99re caught out. We must be virtuous.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I move we go to Mother Yeo\xe2\x80\x99s for a last gorge. Let\xe2\x80\x99s try Mother Yeo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey sought a little bottle-windowed half dairy, half restaurant, a\r\ndark-brewed, two-hundred-year-old house, at the head of a narrow side\r\nstreet. They had patronized it from the days of their fagdom, and were\r\nvery much friends at home.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ve come to pay our debts, mother,\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky, sliding his arm\r\nround the fifty-six-inch waist of the mistress of the establishment. \xe2\x80\x9cTo\r\npay our debts and say good-by--and--and we\xe2\x80\x99re awf\xe2\x80\x99ly hungry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAie!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mother Yeo, \xe2\x80\x9cmakkin\xe2\x80\x99 love to me! I\xe2\x80\x99m shaamed of \xe2\x80\x99ee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Rackon us wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t du no such thing if Mary was here,\xe2\x80\x9d said McTurk,\r\nlapsing into the broad North Devon that the boys used on their\r\ncampaigns.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho\xe2\x80\x99m takin\xe2\x80\x99 my name in vain?\xe2\x80\x9d The inner door opened, and Mary,\r\nfair-haired, blue-eyed, and apple-checked, entered with a bowl of cream\r\nin her hands. McTurk kissed her. Beetle followed suit, with exemplary\r\ncalm. Both boys were promptly cuffed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNiver kiss the maid when \xe2\x80\x99e can kiss the mistress,\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky,\r\nshamelessly winking at Mother Yeo, as he investigated a shelf of jams.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGlad to see one of \xe2\x80\x99ee don\xe2\x80\x99t want his head slapped no more?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary\r\ninvitingly, in that direction.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNeu! Reckon I can get \xe2\x80\x99em give me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky, his back turned.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot by me--yeou little masterpiece!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNiver asked \xe2\x80\x99ee. There\xe2\x80\x99s maids to Northam. Yiss--an\xe2\x80\x99 Appledore.\xe2\x80\x9d An\r\nunreproducible sniff, half contempt, half reminiscence, rounded the\r\nretort.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAie! With\r\nhis house-cap on!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTime, 3.57 p.m. Make a note o\xe2\x80\x99 that. What d\xe2\x80\x99you mean, Beetle?\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nStalky.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell! The little loft behind Randall\xe2\x80\x99s printing office was his own\r\nterritory, where he saw himself already controlling the \xe2\x80\x9cTimes.\xe2\x80\x9d Here,\r\nunder the guidance of the inky apprentice, he had learned to find his\r\nway more or less circuitously about the case, and considered himself an\r\nexpert compositor.\r\n\r\nThe school paper in its locked formes lay on a stone-topped table, a\r\nproof by the side; but not for worlds would Beetle have corrected from\r\nthe mere proof. With a mallet and a pair of tweezers, he knocked out\r\nmysterious wedges of wood that released the forme, picked a letter here\r\nand inserted a letter there, reading as he went along and stopping much\r\nto chuckle over his own contributions.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou won\xe2\x80\x99t show off like that,\xe2\x80\x9d said McTurk, \xe2\x80\x9cwhen you\xe2\x80\x99ve got to do it\r\nfor your living. What a lark!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThink o\xe2\x80\x99 the pure-souled, high-minded boys who\xe2\x80\x99d give their eyes for a\r\nsquint at it!\xe2\x80\x9d said McTurk.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, Willie dear,\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky; \xe2\x80\x9cthat would be wrong and painful to our\r\nkind teachers. Here\xe2\x80\x99s three lines\r\nthat can move up all in a lump.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98One of those scientific rests for which this eminent huntsman is so\r\njustly celebrated.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d Stalky knew the Puffington run by heart.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHold on! But\r\nthe revision of the classics had detained them too long. They halted,\r\nblown and breathless, in the furze at the back of the gasometer, the\r\nCollege lights twinkling below, ten minutes at least late for tea and\r\nlock-up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s no good,\xe2\x80\x9d puffed McTurk. \xe2\x80\x9cBet a bob Foxy is waiting for defaulters\r\nunder the lamp by the Fives Court. Two hundred and fifty boys in high\r\ncondition, seeking for more light, are truly earnest inquirers.\r\n\r\nWhen a most vile smell of gas told them that supplies had been renewed,\r\nStalky, waistcoat unbuttoned, sat gorgedly over what might have been\r\nhis fourth cup of tea. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd that\xe2\x80\x99s all right,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cHullo! \xe2\x80\x99Ere\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nPomponius Ego!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was Carson, the head of the school, a simple, straight-minded soul,\r\nand a pillar of the First Fifteen, who crossed over from the prefects\xe2\x80\x99\r\ntable and in a husky, official voice invited the three to attend in his\r\nstudy in half an hour. \xe2\x80\x9cPrefects\xe2\x80\x99 meetin\xe2\x80\x99!
