All your fault, Tulke.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I didn\xe2\x80\x99t kiss her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou ass! If you\xe2\x80\x99d said you _had_ and stuck to it, it would have been\r\nten times better than what you did,\xe2\x80\x9d Naughten retorted. \xe2\x80\x9cNow they\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\ntell the whole school--and Beetle\xe2\x80\x99ll make up a lot of beastly rhymes and\r\nnick-names.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, hang it, she kissed me!\xe2\x80\x9d Outside of his work, Tulke\xe2\x80\x99s mind moved\r\nslowly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not thinking of you. I\xe2\x80\x99m thinking of us. I\xe2\x80\x99ll go up to their study\r\nand see if I can make \xe2\x80\x99em keep quiet!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTulke\xe2\x80\x99s awf\xe2\x80\x99ly cut up about this business,\xe2\x80\x9d Naughten began,\r\ningratiatingly, when he found Beetle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho\xe2\x80\x99s kissed him this time?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c--and I\xe2\x80\x99ve come to ask you chaps, and especially you, Beetle, not to\r\nlet the thing be known all over the school. Of course, fellows as senior\r\nas you are can easily see why.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUm!\xe2\x80\x9d said Beetle, with the cold reluctance of one who foresees an\r\nunpleasant public duty. \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose I must go and talk to the Sixth\r\nagain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot the least need, my dear chap, I assure you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Naughten hastily.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll take any message you care to send.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut the chance of supplying the missing adjective was too tempting. So\r\nNaughten returned to that still undissolved meeting, Beetle, white, icy,\r\nand aloof, at his heels.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere seems,\xe2\x80\x9d he began, with laboriously crisp articulation, \xe2\x80\x9cthere\r\nseems to be a certain amount of uneasiness among you as to the steps\r\nwe may think fit to take in regard to this last revelation of\r\nthe--ah--obscene. If it is any consolation to you to know that we have\r\ndecided--for the honor of the school, you understand--to keep our mouths\r\nshut as to these--ah--obscenities, you--ah--have it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe wheeled, his head among the stars, and strode statelily back to his\r\nstudy, where Stalky and McTurk lay side by side upon the table wiping\r\ntheir tearful eyes--too weak to move.\r\n\r\n       *       *       *       *       *\r\n\r\nThe Latin prose paper was a success beyond their wildest dreams.\r\nStalky and McTurk were, of course, out of all examinations (they did\r\nextra-tuition with the Head), but Beetle attended with zeal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis, I presume, is a par-ergon on your part,\xe2\x80\x9d said King, as he dealt\r\nout the papers. \xe2\x80\x9cOne final exhibition ere you are translated to loftier\r\nspheres? A last attack on the classics? It seems to confound you\r\nalready.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBeetle studied the print with knit brows. \xe2\x80\x9c_I_ can\xe2\x80\x99t make head or tail\r\nof it,\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat does it mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no!\xe2\x80\x9d said King, with scholastic coquetry. \xe2\x80\x9cWe depend upon _you_\r\nto give us the meaning. This is an examination, Beetle mine, not a\r\nguessing-competition. You will find your associates have no difficulty\r\nin--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTulke left his place and laid the paper on the desk. King looked, read,\r\nand turned a ghastly green.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStalky\xe2\x80\x99s missing a heap,\xe2\x80\x9d thought Beetle. \xe2\x80\x9cWonder how King\xe2\x80\x99ll get out\r\nof it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere seems,\xe2\x80\x9d King began with a gulp, \xe2\x80\x9ca certain modicum of truth\r\nin our Beetle\xe2\x80\x99s remark. I am--er--inclined to believe that the worthy\r\nRandall must have dropped this in ferule--if you know what that means.\r\nBeetle, you purport to be an editor. Perhaps you can enlighten the form\r\nas to formes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat, sir! Whose form! I don\xe2\x80\x99t see that there\xe2\x80\x99s any verb in this\r\nsentence at all, an\xe2\x80\x99--an\xe2\x80\x99--the Ode is all different, somehow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was about to say, before you volunteered your criticism, that an\r\naccident must have befallen the paper in type, and that the printer\r\nreset it by the light of nature. No--\xe2\x80\x9d he held the thing at arm\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nlength--\xe2\x80\x9cour Randall is not an authority on Cicero or Horace.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRather mean to shove it off on Randall,\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Beetle to his\r\nneighbor. \xe2\x80\x9cKing must ha\xe2\x80\x99 been as screwed as an owl when he wrote it\r\nout.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut we can amend the error by dictating it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d The answer came pat from a dozen throats at once. \xe2\x80\x9cThat cuts\r\nthe time for the exam. Only two hours allowed, sir. \xe2\x80\x99Tisn\xe2\x80\x99t fair. It\xe2\x80\x99s\r\na printed-paper exam. How\xe2\x80\x99re we goin\xe2\x80\x99 to be marked for it! It\xe2\x80\x99s all\r\nRandall\xe2\x80\x99s fault. It isn\xe2\x80\x99t _our_ fault, anyhow. An exam.\xe2\x80\x99s an exam.,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\netc., etc.\r\n\r\nNaturally Mr. King considered this was an attempt to undermine his\r\nauthority, and, instead of beginning dictation at once, delivered a\r\nlecture on the spirit in which examinations should be approached. As the\r\nstorm subsided, Beetle fanned it afresh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEh? What? What was that you were saying to MacLagan?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI only said I thought the papers ought to have been looked at before\r\nthey were given out, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHear, hear!\xe2\x80\x9d from a back bench. Mr. King wished to know whether Beetle\r\ntook it upon himself personally to conduct the traditions of the school.\r\nHis zeal for knowledge ate up another fifteen minutes, during which the\r\nprefects showed unmistakable signs of boredom.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it was a giddy time,\xe2\x80\x9d said Beetle, afterwards, in dismantled Number\r\nFive. \xe2\x80\x9cHe gibbered a bit, and I kept him on the gibber, and then he\r\ndictated about a half of Dolabella & Co.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood old Dolabella! Friend of mine. Yes?\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky, pensively.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen we had to ask him how every other word was spelt, of course, and\r\nhe gibbered a lot more. He cursed me and MacLagan (Mac played up like a\r\ntrump) and Randall, and the \xe2\x80\x98materialized ignorance of the unscholarly\r\nmiddle classes,\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98lust for mere marks,\xe2\x80\x99 and all the rest. It was what\r\nyou might call a final exhibition--a last attack--a giddy par-ergon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut o\xe2\x80\x99 course he was blind squiffy when he wrote the paper. I hope you\r\nexplained _that_?\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes. I told Tulke so. I said an immoral prefect an\xe2\x80\x99 a drunken\r\nhouse-master were legitimate inferences. Tulke nearly blubbed. He\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nawfully shy of us since Mary\xe2\x80\x99s time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTulke preserved that modesty till the last moment--till the\r\njourney-money had been paid, and the boys were filling the brakes that\r\ntook them to the station. Then the three tenderly constrained him to\r\nwait a while.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou see, Tulke, you may be a prefect,\xe2\x80\x9d said Stalky, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I\xe2\x80\x99ve left the\r\nColl. Do you see, Tulke, dear?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I see. Don\xe2\x80\x99t bear malice, Stalky.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStalky? Curse your impudence, you young cub,\xe2\x80\x9d shouted Stalky,\r\nmagnificent in top-hat, stiff collar, spats, and high-waisted,\r\nsnuff-colored ulster. \xe2\x80\x9cI want you to understand that _I\xe2\x80\x99m_ Mister\r\nCorkran, an\xe2\x80\x99 you\xe2\x80\x99re a dirty little schoolboy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBesides bein\xe2\x80\x99 frabjously immoral,\xe2\x80\x9d said McTurk. \xe2\x80\x9cWonder you aren\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nashamed to foist your company on pure-minded boys like us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome on, Tulke,\xe2\x80\x99 cried Naughten, from the prefects\xe2\x80\x99 brake.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, we\xe2\x80\x99re comin\xe2\x80\x99. Shove up and make room, you Collegers. You\xe2\x80\x99ve all\r\ngot to be back next term, with your \xe2\x80\x98Yes, sir,\xe2\x80\x99 and \xe2\x80\x98Oh, sir,\xe2\x80\x99 an\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98No\r\nsir\xe2\x80\x99 an\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98Please sir\xe2\x80\x99; but before we say good-by we\xe2\x80\x99re going to tell you\r\na little story. Go on, Dickie\xe2\x80\x9d (this to the driver); \xe2\x80\x9cwe\xe2\x80\x99re quite ready.\r\nKick that hat-box under the seat, an\xe2\x80\x99 don\xe2\x80\x99t crowd your Uncle Stalky.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs nice a lot of high-minded youngsters as you\xe2\x80\x99d wish to see,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMcTurk, gazing round with bland patronage. \xe2\x80\x9cA trifle immoral, but\r\nthen--boys will be boys. It\xe2\x80\x99s no good tryin\xe2\x80\x99 to look stuffy, Carson.\r\n_Mister_ Corkran will now oblige with the story of Tulke an\xe2\x80\x99 Mary Yeo!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nSLAVES OF THE LAMP.\r\n\r\nPart II.\r\n\r\n\r\nThat very Infant who told the story of the capture of Boh Na Ghee\r\n[_A Conference_ _of the Powers_: \xe2\x80\x9cMany Inventions\xe2\x80\x9d] to Eustace Cleaver,\r\nnovelist, inherited an estateful baronetcy, with vast revenues, resigned\r\nthe service, and became a landholder, while his mother stood guard over\r\nhim to see that he married the right girl. But, new to his position, he\r\npresented the local volunteers with a full-sized magazine-rifle range,\r\ntwo miles long, across the heart of his estate, and the surrounding\r\nfamilies, who lived in savage seclusion among woods full of pheasants,\r\nregarded him as an erring maniac. The noise of the firing disturbed\r\ntheir poultry, and Infant was cast out from the society of J.P.\xe2\x80\x99s and\r\ndecent men till such time as a daughter of the county might lure him\r\nback to right thinking. He took his revenge by filling the house\r\nwith choice selections of old schoolmates home on leave--affable\r\ndetrimentals, at whom the bicycle-riding maidens of the surrounding\r\nfamilies were allowed to look from afar. I knew when a troop-ship was in\r\nport by the Infant\xe2\x80\x99s invitations. Sometimes he would produce old friends\r\nof equal seniority; at others, young and blushing giants whom I had left\r\nsmall fags far down in the Lower Second; and to these Infant and the\r\nelders expounded the whole duty of man in the Army.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve had to cut the service,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Infant; \xe2\x80\x9cbut that\xe2\x80\x99s no reason\r\nwhy my vast stores of experience should be lost to posterity.\xe2\x80\x9d He was\r\njust thirty, and in that same summer an imperious wire drew me to his\r\nbaronial castle: \xe2\x80\x9cGot good haul; ex _Tamar_. Come along.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was an unusually good haul, arranged with a single eye to my benefit.\r\nThere was a baldish, broken-down captain of Native Infantry, shivering\r\nwith ague behind an indomitable red nose--and they called him Captain\r\nDickson. There was another captain, also of Native Infantry, with a fair\r\nmustache; his face was like white glass, and his hands were fragile, but\r\nhe answered joyfully to the cry of Tertius. There was an enormously\r\nbig and well-kept man, who had evidently not campaigned for years,\r\nclean-shaved, soft-voiced, and cat-like, but still Abanazar for all that\r\nhe adorned the Indian Political Service; and there was a lean Irishman,\r\nhis face tanned blue-black with the suns of the Telegraph Department.\r\nLuckily the baize doors of the bachelors\xe2\x80\x99 wing fitted tight, for we\r\ndressed promiscuously in the corridor or in each other\xe2\x80\x99s rooms, talking,\r\ncalling, shouting, and anon waltzing by pairs to songs of Dick Four\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nown devising.\r\n\r\nThere were sixty years of mixed work to be sifted out between us,\r\nand since we had met one another from time to time in the quick\r\nscene-shifting of India--a dinner, camp, or a race-meeting here; a\r\ndak-bungalow or railway station up country somewhere else--we had never\r\nquite lost touch. Infant sat on the banisters, hungrily and enviously\r\ndrinking it in. He enjoyed his baronetcy, but his heart yearned for the\r\nold days.\r\n\r\nIt was a cheerful babel of matters personal, provincial, and imperial,\r\npieces of old call-over lists, and new policies, cut short by the roar\r\nof a Burmese gong, and we went down not less than a quarter of a mile of\r\nstairs to meet Infant\xe2\x80\x99s mother, who had known us all in our school-days\r\nand greeted us as if those had ended a week ago. But it was\r\nfifteen years since, with tears of laughter, she had lent me a gray\r\nprincess-skirt for amateur theatricals.\r\n\r\nThat was a dinner from the \xe2\x80\x9cArabian Nights,\xe2\x80\x9d served in an eighty-foot\r\nhall full of ancestors and pots of flowering roses, and, what was more\r\nimpressive, heated by steam. When it was ended and the little mother had\r\ngone away--(\xe2\x80\x9cYou boys want to talk, so I shall say good-night now\xe2\x80\x9d)--we\r\ngathered about an apple-wood fire, in a gigantic polished steel grate,\r\nunder a mantel-piece ten feet high, and the Infant compassed us about\r\nwith curious liqueurs and that kind of cigarette which serves best to\r\nintroduce your own pipe.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, bliss!\xe2\x80\x9d grunted Dick Four from a sofa, where he had been packed\r\nwith a rug over him. \xe2\x80\x9cFirst time I\xe2\x80\x99ve been warm since I came home.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe were all nearly on top of the fire, except Infant, who had been long\r\nenough at home to take exercise when he felt chilled. This is a grisly\r\ndiversion, but much affected by the English of the Island.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you say a word about cold tubs and brisk walks,\xe2\x80\x9d drawled McTurk,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll kill you, Infant. I\xe2\x80\x99ve got a liver, too. \xe2\x80\x99Member when we used\r\nto think it a treat to turn out of our beds on a Sunday\r\nmorning--thermometer fifty-seven degrees if it was summer--and bathe off\r\nthe Pebbleridge? Ugh!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Thing I don\xe2\x80\x99t understand,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tertius, \xe2\x80\x9cwas the way we chaps used to\r\ngo down into the lavatories, boil ourselves pink, and then come up with\r\nall our pores open into a young snow-storm or a black frost. Yet none of\r\nour chaps died, that I can remember.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTalkin\xe2\x80\x99 of baths,\xe2\x80\x9d said McTurk, with a chuckle, \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99member our bath in\r\nNumber Five, Beetle, the night Rabbits-Eggs rocked King? What wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nI give to see old Stalky now! He is the only one of the two Studies not\r\nhere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStalky is the great man of his Century,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick Four.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow d\xe2\x80\x99you know?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do I know?\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick Four, scornfully. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you\xe2\x80\x99ve ever been in a\r\ntight place with Stalky you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t ask.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI haven\xe2\x80\x99t seen him since the camp at Pindi in \xe2\x80\x9987,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cHe was\r\ngoin\xe2\x80\x99 strong then--about seven feet high and four feet through.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAdequate chap. Infernally adequate,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tertius, pulling his mustache\r\nand staring into the fire.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGot dam\xe2\x80\x99 near court-martialed and broke in Egypt in \xe2\x80\x9984,\xe2\x80\x9d the Infant\r\nvolunteered. \xe2\x80\x9cI went out in the same trooper with him--as raw as he was.\r\nOnly _I_ showed it, and Stalky didn\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat was the trouble?\xe2\x80\x9d said McTurk, reaching forward absently to twitch\r\nmy dress-tie into position.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, nothing. His colonel trusted him to take twenty Tommies out to\r\nwash, or groom camels, or something at the back of Suakin, and Stalky\r\ngot embroiled with Fuzzies five miles in the interior. He conducted a\r\nmasterly retreat and wiped up eight of \xe2\x80\x99em. He knew jolly well he\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nno right to go out so far, so he took the initiative and pitched in a\r\nletter to his colonel, who was frothing at the mouth, complaining of the\r\n\xe2\x80\x99paucity of support accorded to him in his operations.\xe2\x80\x99 Gad, it might\r\nhave been one fat brigadier slangin\xe2\x80\x99 another! Then he went into the\r\nStaff Corps.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat--is--entirely--Stalky,\xe2\x80\x9d said Abanazar from his arm-chair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve come across him, too?\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied in his softest tones. \xe2\x80\x9cI was at the tail of\r\nthat--that epic. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you chaps know?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe did not--Infant, McTurk, and I; and we called for information very\r\npolitely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Twasn\xe2\x80\x99t anything,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tertius. \xe2\x80\x9cWe got into a mess up in the\r\nKhye-Kheen Hills a couple o\xe2\x80\x99 years ago, and Stalky pulled us through.\r\nThat\xe2\x80\x99s all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMcTurk gazed at Tertius with all an Irishman\xe2\x80\x99s contempt for the\r\ntongue-tied Saxon.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHeavens!\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd it\xe2\x80\x99s you and your likes govern Ireland.\r\nTertius, aren\xe2\x80\x99t you ashamed?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I can\xe2\x80\x99t tell a yarn. I can chip in when the other fellow starts\r\n_bukhing_. Ask him.\xe2\x80\x9d He pointed to Dick Four, whose nose gleamed\r\nscornfully over the rug.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI knew you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick Four. \xe2\x80\x9cGive me a whiskey and soda. I\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nbeen drinking lemon-squash and ammoniated quinine while you chaps were\r\nbathin\xe2\x80\x99 in champagne, and my head\xe2\x80\x99s singin\xe2\x80\x99 like a top.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe wiped his ragged mustache above the drink; and, his teeth chattering\r\nin his head, began: \xe2\x80\x9cYou know the Khye-Kheen-Mal\xc3\xb4t expedition, when\r\nwe scared the souls out of \xe2\x80\x99em with a field force they daren\xe2\x80\x99t fight\r\nagainst? Well, both tribes--there was a coalition against us--came in\r\nwithout firing a shot; and a lot of hairy villains, who had no more\r\npower over their men than I had, promised and vowed all sorts of things.\r\nOn that very slender evidence, Pussy dear--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was at Simla,\xe2\x80\x9d said Abanazar, hastily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind, you\xe2\x80\x99re tarred with the same brush. On the strength of\r\nthose tuppenny-ha\xe2\x80\x99penny treaties, your asses of Politicals reported the\r\ncountry as pacified, and the Government, being a fool, as usual, began\r\nroad-makin\xe2\x80\x99--dependin\xe2\x80\x99 on local supply for labor. \xe2\x80\x99Member _that_, Pussy?\r\n\xe2\x80\x99Rest of our chaps who\xe2\x80\x99d had no look-in during the campaign didn\xe2\x80\x99t think\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x99d be any more of it, and were anxious to get back to India. But\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99d been in two of these little rows before, and I had my suspicions.\r\nI engineered myself, _summa ingenio_, into command of a road-patrol--no\r\nshovellin\xe2\x80\x99, only marching up and down genteelly with a guard. They\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nwithdrawn all the troops they could, but I nucleused about forty\r\nPathans, recruits chiefly, of my regiment, and sat tight at the\r\nbase-camp while the road-parties went to work, as per Political survey.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHad some rippin\xe2\x80\x99 sing-songs in camp, too,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tertius.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy pup\xe2\x80\x9d--thus did Dick Four refer to his subaltern--\xe2\x80\x9cwas a pious\r\nlittle beast. He didn\xe2\x80\x99t like the sing-songs, and so he went down with\r\npneumonia. I rootled round the camp, and found Tertius gassing about as\r\na D.A.Q.M.G., which, God knows, he isn\xe2\x80\x99t cut out for. There were six\r\nor eight of the old Coll. at base-camp (we\xe2\x80\x99re always in force for a\r\nfrontier row), but I\xe2\x80\x99d heard of Tertius as a steady old hack, and I told\r\nhim he had to shake off his D.A.Q.M.G. breeches and help _me_. Tertius\r\nvolunteered like a shot, and we settled it with the authorities, and out\r\nwe went--forty Pathans, Tertius, and me, looking up the road-parties.\r\nMacnamara\xe2\x80\x99s--\xe2\x80\x99member old Mac, the Sapper, who played the fiddle so\r\ndamnably at Umballa?--Mac\xe2\x80\x99s party was the last but one. The last was\r\nStalky\xe2\x80\x99s. He was at the head of the road with some of his pet Sikhs. Mac\r\nsaid he believed he was all right.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStalky _is_ a Sikh,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tertius. \xe2\x80\x9cHe takes his men to pray at the\r\nDurbar Sahib at Amritzar, regularly as clockwork, when he can.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t interrupt, Tertius. It was about forty miles beyond Mac\xe2\x80\x99s before\r\nI found him; and my men pointed out gently, but firmly, that the country\r\nwas risin\xe2\x80\x99. What kind o\xe2\x80\x99 country, Beetle? Well, _I_\xe2\x80\x99m no word-painter,\r\nthank goodness, but _you_ might call it a hellish country! When we\r\nweren\xe2\x80\x99t up to our necks in snow, we were rolling down the khud. The\r\nwell-disposed inhabitants, who were to supply labor for the road-making\r\n(don\xe2\x80\x99t forget that, Pussy dear), sat behind rocks and took pot-shots\r\nat us. \xe2\x80\x98Old, old story! We all legged it in search of Stalky. I had a\r\nfeeling that he\xe2\x80\x99d be in good cover, and about dusk we found him and his\r\nroad-party, as snug as a bug in a rug, in an old Mal\xc3\xb4t stone fort, with\r\na watch-tower at one corner. It overhung the road they had blasted\r\nout of the cliff fifty feet below; and under the road things went down\r\npretty sheer, for five or six hundred feet, into a gorge about half a\r\nmile wide and two or three miles long. There were chaps on the other\r\nside of the gorge scientifically gettin\xe2\x80\x99 our range. So I hammered on\r\nthe gate and nipped in, and tripped over Stalky in a greasy, bloody old\r\nposhteen, squatting on the ground, eating with his men. I\xe2\x80\x99d only seen\r\nhim for half a minute about three months before, but I might have met\r\nhim yesterday. He waved his hand all sereno.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Hullo, Aladdin! Hullo, Emperor!\xe2\x80\x99 he said. \xe2\x80\x98You\xe2\x80\x99re just in time for the\r\nperformance.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI saw his Sikhs looked a bit battered. \xe2\x80\x98Where\xe2\x80\x99s your command? Where\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nyour subaltern?\xe2\x80\x99 I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Here--all there is of it,\xe2\x80\x99 said Stalky. \xe2\x80\x98If you want young Everett,\r\nhe\xe2\x80\x99s dead, and his body\xe2\x80\x99s in the watch-tower. They rushed our road-party\r\nlast week, and got him and seven men. We\xe2\x80\x99ve been besieged for five days.\r\nI suppose they let you through to make sure of you. The whole country\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nup. \xe2\x80\x99Strikes me you\xe2\x80\x99ve walked into a first-class trap.\xe2\x80\x99 He grinned, but\r\nneither Tertius nor I could see where the deuce the fun was. We hadn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nany grub for our men, and Stalky had only four days\xe2\x80\x99 whack for his. That\r\ncame of dependin\xe2\x80\x99 upon your asinine Politicals, Pussy dear, who told us\r\nthat the inhabitants were friendly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo make us _quite_ comfy, Stalky took us up to the watch-tower to see\r\npoor Everett\xe2\x80\x99s body, lyin\xe2\x80\x99 in a foot o\xe2\x80\x99 drifted snow. It looked like a\r\ngirl of fifteen--not a hair on the little fellow\xe2\x80\x99s face. He\xe2\x80\x99d been shot\r\nthrough the temple, but the Mal\xc3\xb4ts had left their mark on him. Stalky\r\nunbuttoned the tunic, and showed it to us--a rummy sickle-shaped cut on\r\nthe chest. \xe2\x80\x99Member the snow all white on his eyebrows, Tertius? \xe2\x80\x99Member\r\nwhen Stalky moved the lamp and it looked as if he was alive?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYe-es,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tertius, with a shudder. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Member the beastly look on\r\nStalky\xe2\x80\x99s face, though, with his nostrils all blown out, same as he used\r\nto look when he was bullyin\xe2\x80\x99 a fag? That was a lovely evening.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe held a council of war up there over Everett\xe2\x80\x99s body. Stalky said the\r\nMal\xc3\xb4ts and Khye-Kheens were up together; havin\xe2\x80\x99 sunk their blood feuds\r\nto settle us. The chaps we\xe2\x80\x99d seen across the gorge were Khye-Kheens. It\r\nwas about half a mile from them to us as a bullet flies, and they\xe2\x80\x99d made\r\na line of sungars under the brow of the hill to sleep in and starve us\r\nout. The Mal\xc3\xb4ts, he said, were in front of us promiscuous. There wasn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ngood cover behind the fort, or they\xe2\x80\x99d have been there, too. Stalky\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t mind the Mal\xc3\xb4ts half as much as he did the Khye-Kheens. He said\r\nthe Mal\xc3\xb4ts were treacherous curs. What I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t understand was, why\r\nin the world the two gangs didn\xe2\x80\x99t join in and rush us. There must have\r\nbeen at least five hundred of \xe2\x80\x99em. Stalky said they didn\xe2\x80\x99t trust each\r\nother very well, because they were ancestral enemies when they were\r\nat home; and the only time they\xe2\x80\x99d tried a rush he\xe2\x80\x99d hove a couple of\r\nblasting-charges among \xe2\x80\x99em, and that had sickened \xe2\x80\x99em a bit.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was dark by the time we finished, and Stalky, always serene, said:\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You command now. I don\xe2\x80\x99t suppose you mind my taking any action I may\r\nconsider necessary to reprovision the fort?\xe2\x80\x99 I said, \xe2\x80\x98Of course not,\xe2\x80\x99\r\nand then the lamp blew out. So Tertius and I had to climb down the tower\r\nsteps (we didn\xe2\x80\x99t want to stay with Everett) and got back to our men.\r\nStalky had gone off--to count the stores, I supposed. Anyhow, Tertius\r\nand I sat up in case of a rush (they were plugging at us pretty\r\ngenerally, you know), relieving each other till the mornin\xe2\x80\x99.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMornin\xe2\x80\x99 came. No Stalky. Not a sign of him. I took counsel with his\r\nsenior native officer--a grand, white-whiskered old chap--Rutton Singh,\r\nfrom Jullunder-way. He only grinned, and said it was all right. Stalky\r\nhad been out of the fort twice before, somewhere or other, accordin\xe2\x80\x99 to\r\nhim. He said Stalky \xe2\x80\x99ud come back unchipped, and gave me to understand\r\nthat Stalky was an invulnerable _Guru_ of sorts. All the same, I put the\r\nwhole command on half rations, and set \xe2\x80\x99em to pickin\xe2\x80\x99 out loopholes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbout noon there was no end of a snow-storm, and the enemy stopped\r\nfiring. We replied gingerly, because we were awfully short of\r\nammunition. Don\xe2\x80\x99t suppose we fired five shots an hour, but we generally\r\ngot our man. Well, while I was talking with Rutton Singh I saw Stalky\r\ncoming down from the watch-tower, rather puffy about the eyes, his\r\nposhteen coated with claret-colored ice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98No trustin\xe2\x80\x99 these snow-storms,\xe2\x80\x99 he said. \xe2\x80\x98Nip out quick and snaffle\r\nwhat you can get. There\xe2\x80\x99s a certain amount of friction between the\r\nKhye-Kheens and the Mal\xc3\xb4ts just now.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI turned Tertius out with twenty Pathans, and they bucked about in the\r\nsnow for a bit till they came on to a sort of camp about eight hundred\r\nyards away, with only a few men in charge and half a dozen sheep by\r\nthe fire. They finished off the men, and snaffled the sheep and as much\r\ngrain as they could carry, and came back. No one fired a shot at \xe2\x80\x99em.\r\nThere didn\xe2\x80\x99t seem to be anybody about, but the snow was falling pretty\r\nthick.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98That\xe2\x80\x99s good enough,\xe2\x80\x99 said Stalky when we got dinner ready and he was\r\nchewin\xe2\x80\x99 mutton-kababs off a cleanin\xe2\x80\x99 rod. \xe2\x80\x98There\xe2\x80\x99s no sense riskin\xe2\x80\x99 men.\r\nThey\xe2\x80\x99re holding a pow-wow between the Khye-Kheens and the Mal\xc3\xb4ts at\r\nthe head of the gorge. I don\xe2\x80\x99t think these so-called coalitions are much\r\ngood.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you know what that maniac had done? Tertius and I shook it out of\r\nhim by instalments. There was an underground granary cellar-room below\r\nthe watch-tower, and in blasting the road Stalky had blown a hole into\r\none side of it. Being no one else _but_ Stalky, he\xe2\x80\x99d kept the hole open\r\nfor his own ends; and laid poor Everett\xe2\x80\x99s body slap over the well of the\r\nstairs that led down to it from the watch-tower. He\xe2\x80\x99d had to move and\r\nreplace the corpse every time he used the passage. The Sikhs wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t go\r\nnear the place, of course. Well, he\xe2\x80\x99d got out of this hole, and dropped\r\non to the road. Then, in the night _and_ a howling snow-storm, he\xe2\x80\x99d\r\ndropped over the edge of the khud, made his way down to the bottom of\r\nthe gorge, forded the nullah, which was half frozen, climbed up on the\r\nother side along a track he\xe2\x80\x99d discovered, and come out on the right\r\nflank of the Khye-Kheens. He had then--listen to this!--crossed over a\r\nridge that paralleled their rear, walked half a mile behind that, and\r\ncome out on the left of their line where the gorge gets shallow and\r\nwhere there was a regular track between the Mal\xc3\xb4t and the Khye-Kheen\r\ncamps. So Stalky abolished him quietly, and left\r\nhim--_with_ the Mal\xc3\xb4t mark on his chest, same as Everett had.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98I was just as economical as I could be,\xe2\x80\x99 Stalky said to us. \xe2\x80\x98If he\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nshouted I should have been slain. I\xe2\x80\x99d never had to do that kind of thing\r\nbut once before, and that was the first time I tried that path. It\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nperfectly practicable for infantry, you know.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98What about your first man?\xe2\x80\x99 I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Oh, that was the night after they killed Everett, and I went out\r\nlookin\xe2\x80\x99 for a line of retreat for my men. A man found me. I abolished\r\nhim--_privatim_--scragged him. But on thinkin\xe2\x80\x99 it over it occurred to\r\nme that if I could find the body (I\xe2\x80\x99d hove it down some rocks) I might\r\ndecorate it with the Mal\xc3\xb4t mark and leave it to the Khye-Kheens to draw\r\ninferences. So I went out again the next night and did. The Khye-Kheens\r\nare shocked at the Mal\xc3\xb4ts perpetratin\xe2\x80\x99 these two dastardly outrages\r\nafter they\xe2\x80\x99d sworn to sink all bleed feuds. I lay up behind their\r\nsungars early this morning and watched \xe2\x80\x99em. They all went to confer\r\nabout it at the head of the gorge. Awf\xe2\x80\x99ly annoyed they are. Don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nwonder.\xe2\x80\x99 You know the way Stalky drops out his words, one by one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy God!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Infant, explosively, as the full depth of the\r\nstrategy dawned on him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear-r man!\xe2\x80\x9d said McTurk, purring rapturously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStalky stalked,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tertius. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s all there is to it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, he didn\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick Four. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you remember how he insisted\r\nthat he had only applied his luck? Don\xe2\x80\x99t you remember how Rutton Singh\r\ngrabbed his boots and grovelled in the snow, and how our men shouted?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNone of our Pathans believed that was luck,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tertius. \xe2\x80\x9cThey swore\r\nStalky ought to have been born a Pathan, and--\xe2\x80\x99member we nearly had a\r\nrow in the fort when Rutton Singh said Stalky was a Pathan? But Stalky just waggled his\r\nfinger and they shut up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOld Rutton Singh\xe2\x80\x99s sword was half out, though, and he swore he\xe2\x80\x99d\r\ncremate every Khye-Kheen and Mal\xc3\xb4t he killed. That made the Jemadar\r\npretty wild, because he didn\xe2\x80\x99t mind fighting against his own creed, but\r\nhe wasn\xe2\x80\x99t going to crab a fellow Mussulman\xe2\x80\x99s chances of Paradise. Where the deuce\r\ndid he pick up his Pushtu from, Beetle?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind his language, Dick,\xe2\x80\x9d said I. \xe2\x80\x9cGive us the gist of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI flatter myself I can address the wily Pathan on occasion, but, hang\r\nit all, I can\xe2\x80\x99t make puns in Pushtu, or top off my arguments with a\r\nsmutty story, as he did. He played on those two old dogs o\xe2\x80\x99 war like\r\na--like a concertina. Stalky said--and the other two backed up his\r\nknowledge of Oriental nature--that the Khye-Kheens and the Mal\xc3\xb4ts\r\nbetween \xe2\x80\x99em would organize a combined attack on us that night, as\r\na proof of good faith. Stalky\xe2\x80\x99s notion was to crawl out at dusk with\r\nhis Sikhs, manoeuvre \xe2\x80\x99em along this ungodly goat-track that he\xe2\x80\x99d found,\r\nto the back of the Khye-Kheen position, and then lob in a few long shots\r\nat the Mal\xc3\xb4ts when the attack was well on. \xe2\x80\x98That\xe2\x80\x99ll divert their minds\r\nand help to agitate \xe2\x80\x99em,\xe2\x80\x99 he said. \xe2\x80\x98Then you chaps can come out and\r\nsweep up the pieces, and we\xe2\x80\x99ll rendezvous at the head of the gorge.\r\nAfter that, I move we get back to Mac\xe2\x80\x99s camp and have something to eat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_You_ were commandin\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x9d the Infant suggested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was about three months senior to Stalky, and two months Tertius\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsenior,\xe2\x80\x9d Dick Four replied. \xe2\x80\x9c_But_ we were all from the same old Coll.\r\nI should say ours was the only little affair on record where some one\r\nwasn\xe2\x80\x99t jealous of some one else.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_We_ weren\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d Tertius broke in, \xe2\x80\x9cbut there was another row between Gul\r\nSher Khan and Rutton Singh. Our Jemadar said--he was quite right--that\r\nno Sikh living could stalk worth a damn; and that Koran Sahib had better\r\ntake out the Pathans, who understood that kind of mountain work. Rutton\r\nSingh said that Koran Sahib jolly well knew every Pathan was a born\r\ndeserter, and every Sikh was a gentleman, even if he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t crawl on\r\nhis belly. Stalky struck in with some woman\xe2\x80\x99s proverb or other, that had\r\nthe effect of doublin\xe2\x80\x99 both men up with a grin. He said the Sikhs and\r\nthe Pathans could settle their claims on the Khye-Kheens and\r\nMal\xc3\xb4ts later on, but he was going to take his Sikhs along for this\r\nmountain-climbing job, because Sikhs could shoot. They can, too. Give\r\n\xe2\x80\x99em a mule-load of ammunition apiece, and they\xe2\x80\x99re perfectly happy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd out he gat,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick Four. \xe2\x80\x9cAs soon as it was dark, and he\xe2\x80\x99d had\r\na bit of a snooze, him and thirty Sikhs went down through the staircase\r\nin the tower, every mother\xe2\x80\x99s son of \xe2\x80\x99em salutin\xe2\x80\x99 little Everett where\r\nIt stood propped up against the wall. The last I heard him say was,\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Kubbadar! tumbleinga! [Look out; you\xe2\x80\x99ll fall!] and they tumbleingaed\r\nover the black edge of nothing. Close upon 9 p.m. the combined attack\r\ndeveloped; Khye-Kheens across the valley, and Mal\xc3\xb4ts in front of us,\r\npluggin\xe2\x80\x99 at long range and yellin\xe2\x80\x99 to each other to come along and cut\r\nour infidel throats. Then they skirmished up to the gate, and began the\r\nold game of calling our Pathans renegades, and invitin\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x99em to join the\r\nholy war. One of our men, a young fellow from Dera Ismail, jumped on\r\nthe wall to slang \xe2\x80\x99em back, and jumped down, blubbing like a child. He\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nbeen hit smack in the middle of the hand. \xe2\x80\x98Never saw a man yet who could\r\nstand a hit in the hand without weepin\xe2\x80\x99 bitterly. It tickles up all the\r\nnerves. So Tertius took his rifle and smote the others on the head to\r\nkeep them quiet at the loopholes. The dear children wanted to open the\r\ngate and go in at \xe2\x80\x99em generally, but that didn\xe2\x80\x99t suit our book.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt last, near midnight, I heard the wop, wop, wop, of Stalky\xe2\x80\x99s Martinis\r\nacross the valley, and some general cursing among the Mal\xc3\xb4ts, whose\r\nmain body was hid from us by a fold in the hillside. Stalky was brownin\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\xe2\x80\x99em at a great rate, and very naturally they turned half right and began\r\nto blaze at their faithless allies, the Khye-Kheens--regular volley\r\nfirin\xe2\x80\x99. In less than ten minutes after Stalky opened the diversion they\r\nwere going it hammer and tongs, both sides the valley. When we could\r\nsee, the valley was rather a mixed-up affair. The Khye-Kheens had\r\nstreamed out of their sungars above the gorge to chastise the Mal\xc3\xb4ts,\r\nand Stalky--I was watching him through my glasses--had slipped in behind\r\n\xe2\x80\x99em. Very good. The Khye-Kheens had to leg it along the hillside up to\r\nwhere the gorge got shallow and they could cross over to the Mal\xc3\xb4ts,\r\nwho were awfully cheered to see the Khye-Kheens taken in the rear.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen it occurred to me to comfort the Khye-Kheens. So I turned out the\r\nwhole command, and we advanced _\xc3\xa0 la pas de charge_, doublin\xe2\x80\x99 up what,\r\nfor the sake of argument, we\xe2\x80\x99ll call the Mal\xc3\xb4ts\xe2\x80\x99 left flank. Even then,\r\nif they\xe2\x80\x99d sunk their differences, they could have eaten us alive;\r\nbut they\xe2\x80\x99d been firin\xe2\x80\x99 at each other half the night, and they went on\r\nfirin\xe2\x80\x99. Queerest thing you ever saw in your born days! As soon as our\r\nmen doubled up to the Mal\xc3\xb4ts, they\xe2\x80\x99d blaze at the Khye-Kheens more\r\nzealously than ever, to show they were on our side, run up the valley\r\na few hundred yards, and halt to fire again. The moment Stalky saw our\r\ngame he duplicated it his side the gorge; and, by Jove! the Khye-Kheens\r\ndid just the same thing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tertius, \xe2\x80\x9cyou\xe2\x80\x99ve forgot him playin\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x99Arrah, Patsy, mind\r\nthe baby\xe2\x80\x99 on the bugle to hurry us up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid he?\xe2\x80\x9d roared McTurk. Somehow we all began to sing it, and there was\r\nan interruption.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRather,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tertius, when we were quiet. No one of the Aladdin\r\ncompany could forget that tune. \xe2\x80\x9cYes, he played \xe2\x80\x98Patsy.\xe2\x80\x99 Go on, Dick.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFinally,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick Four, \xe2\x80\x9cwe drove both mobs into each other\xe2\x80\x99s arms on\r\na bit of level ground at the head of the valley, and saw the whole crew\r\nwhirl off, fightin\xe2\x80\x99 and stabbin\xe2\x80\x99 and swearin\xe2\x80\x99 in a blindin\xe2\x80\x99 snow-storm.\r\nThey were a heavy, hairy lot, and we didn\xe2\x80\x99t follow \xe2\x80\x99em.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStalky had captured one prisoner--an old pensioned Sepoy of twenty-five\r\nyears\xe2\x80\x99 service, who produced his discharge--an awf\xe2\x80\x99ly sportin\xe2\x80\x99 old card.\r\nHe had been tryin\xe2\x80\x99 to make his men rush us early in the day. He was\r\nsulky--angry with his own side for their cowardice, and Rutton Singh\r\nwanted to bayonet him--Sikhs don\xe2\x80\x99t understand fightin\xe2\x80\x99 against the\r\nGovernment after you\xe2\x80\x99ve served it honestly--but Stalky rescued him, and\r\nfroze on to him tight--with ulterior motives, I believe. When we got\r\nback to the fort, we buried young Everett--Stalky wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t hear of\r\nblowin\xe2\x80\x99 up the place--and bunked. We\xe2\x80\x99d only lost ten men, all told.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly ten, out of seventy. How did you lose \xe2\x80\x99em?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, there was a rush on the fort early in the night, and a few Mal\xc3\xb4ts\r\ngot over the gate. It was rather a tight thing for a minute or two, but\r\nthe recruits took it beautifully. Lucky job we hadn\xe2\x80\x99t any badly wounded\r\nmen to carry, because we had forty miles to Macnamara\xe2\x80\x99s camp. By Jove,\r\nhow we legged it! Half way in, old Rutton Singh collapsed, so we slung\r\nhim across four rifles and Stalky\xe2\x80\x99s overcoat; and Stalky, his prisoner,\r\nand a couple of Sikhs were his bearers. After that I went to sleep. You\r\ncan, you know, on the march, when your legs get properly numbed. Mac\r\nswears we all marched into his camp snoring and dropped where we halted.\r\nHis men lugged us into the tents like gram-bags. I remember wakin\xe2\x80\x99 up\r\nand seeing Stalky asleep with his head on old Rutton Singh\xe2\x80\x99s chest. _He_\r\nslept twenty-four hours. I only slept seventeen, but then I was coming\r\ndown with dysentery.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cComing down? What rot! You see I was putrid with\r\ndysentery, Tertius was ravin\xe2\x80\x99, half the men had frost-bite, and\r\nMacnamara\xe2\x80\x99s orders were to break camp and come in before winter. That must have been one of Rutton Singh\xe2\x80\x99s villages,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick\r\nFour; and we smoked for some time in silence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say,\xe2\x80\x9d said McTurk, casting back through the years, \xe2\x80\x9cdid Stalky ever\r\ntell you _how_ Rabbits-Eggs came to rock King that night?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick Four. Then McTurk told. \xe2\x80\x9cI see,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick Four,\r\nnodding. \xe2\x80\x9cPractically he duplicated that trick over again. There\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nnobody like Stalky.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s just where you make the mistake,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cIndia\xe2\x80\x99s full of\r\nStalkies--Cheltenham and Haileybury and Marlborough chaps--that we don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nknow anything about, and the surprises will begin when there is really a\r\nbig row on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho will be surprised?\xe2\x80\x9d said Dick Four.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe other side. The gentlemen who go to the front in first-class\r\ncarriages. Just imagine Stalky let loose on the south side of Europe\r\nwith a sufficiency of Sikhs and a reasonable prospect of loot. Consider\r\nit quietly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s something in that, but you\xe2\x80\x99re too much of an optimist, Beetle,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said the Infant.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x99ve a right to be. Ain\xe2\x80\x99t I responsible for the whole thing? You\r\nneedn\xe2\x80\x99t laugh. Who wrote \xe2\x80\x98Aladdin now has got his wife\xe2\x80\x99--eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s that got to do with it?\xe2\x80\x9d said Tertius.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEverything,\xe2\x80\x9d said I.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cProve it,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Infant.\r\n\r\nAnd I have.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nEnd of the Project Gutenberg EBook of Stalky & Co., by Rudyard Kipling\r\n\r\n*** END OF THIS PROJECT GUTENBERG EBOOK STALKY & CO. ***\r\n\r\n***** This file should be named 3006-0.txt or 3006-0.zip *****\r\nThis and all associated files of various formats will be found in:\r\n        http://www.gutenberg.org/3/0/0/3006/\r\n\r\nProduced by A.
